3.20         Technical feasibility and efficiency Is subject to the aorms and stdards established
by the federal and state agencies. For example, the CNA would follow the same technical rules for
fesibility studies and project screening for all projec, independently of the source of financing.
Every project has to be reviewed and approved by the televant sector agency (in Guias Kara I
jlaboracion de Expedientes Tecnicos Simnliflcados, Annex to the CUD Operational Manual) and the
sectoral committee of the COPLADE. The Bank reviewed the corresponding technical manuals and
found then. satisfactory.
3.21         The economic efficiency criteria require at least a 12 percent rate of return for
productive and large infrastructure projects and that social sector and small infrastructure projecs
must minimize costs and respond to revealed social demand, as well as being appropriately targeted to
the poor. In the case of social sector projects, basic technical norms, as defined by the Secretariat of
Public Education (SEP) or CNA, seek to minimize cost per social demand. These minimum costs are
incorporated into the guidelines for project selection and into the scoring system for small projects.
3.22         Operational manuals issued for the Municipal Funds already incorporate the system
for appraisal and selection of small projects described in Annex 4. The final draft of the CUD and
Municipal Funds Operational Manuals has been prepared in close collaboration with the Bank.
Formal approval of such documents has been provided to the Bank. The experience with the small
projects selection system of the FMS will be reviewed with the Bank no later than October 31, 1991.
TMe revised system for small project appraisal, agreed with the Bank, would be incorporated in the
CUD Operational Manual by November 1991, to start to be applied to the POA of 1992. By that
date the two systems for channelling monev (CUD and FMS) to small projects would have similar
rules for appraising small projects. In addition, and in order to monitor improvements in analyzing
all medium and large projects, a satisfactory appraisal report of two subprojects (one medium and one
large) in each of the eight program categories to be financed by the Bank, with at least four
subprojects per state, would have to be submitted to the Bank in order to comply with conditions for
disbursement in calendar years 1991, 1992 and 1993.
3.23         Environmental screening and monitoring of investment projects would be carried out
within the framework established by the national environmental law (Ley General de Equilibrio
Ecologico y Proteccion al Ambiente, 1988) and its regulations, as well as the Ley de Obra Publica
(art. 28, in particular). Projects below US$200,000 will be subjected to a simple environmental
screening process. Larger projects would require an envirownental assessment. Projects with ex-ante
favorable impact (e.g., sewerage, reforestation ) or without environmental impact (e.g., education,
training) would be exempt from this process.' Funds will be provided throughout implementation
to monitor compliance with environmental safeguards established during enviromnental screening and
assessment. SEDUE will be responsible for clearing the annual invesunent programs of each state
and individual subprojects with regard to compliance. The rules to be used for environmental
screening of subprojects are regulated by the above law. During 1991, SEDUE will prepare
guidelines to simplify and improve the application of the above law (see Annex 4, Attachment 3 for
the agreed rules).

(c) EmWnbg ad u ".IiigKimiuL
3.24         The matching grant system used in the CUD has induced a better use of resources and
is an important instrument for ensuring the consistency of national and state objectives. Investment
grants from the federal government are given as matching grants to each project. According to the
CUD Manual, the state share varies from about 75 percent for a productive project in a rich state to
25 percent for a social project in a poor state. Ihis system is applied to the entire PNS (see Annex 5
for the cost sharing in the four states). Ihese rules would also apply under the proposed project.
fii) Own-state and municinal ro
3.25          1hrough the matching grant system, states are required to contribute at least 25
percent of their own resources collected through general taxation within their jurisdiction (Annexes 2
and 3). During project execution it is expected that a similar system, t ing into account the poverty
level of the municipality, will be designed and implemented between each state and its municipalities
for projects with local impact. According to projections presented in Annex 3, Attachment 1, states
will be able to finance the proposed investment program. The 1980 tax reform transferred to the
Federation most of the state and local taxes and introduced a revenue-sharing system through which
about 18 percent of federal taxes are devolved to the states in the form of a block grant. The block
grants represent 70 percent to 80 percent of total revenues of the four states. The remaining 20-30
percent will come from property taxes, water charges, and other minor service charges (derechos and
productos). Urban infrastructure projects also obtain credits from BANOBRAS.
3.26         The project states are being encouraged to improve state tax collection, as referred to
in the Policy Letter. The 1989 revenue-sharing formula between the federation and the states
introduced local taxes as an element in calculating each state's share. Last year, in anticipation of this
change, there was a significant increase in property tax collection (in Guerrero, collections increased
by about 800 percent, and in Hidalgo 600 percent). The project would further help enable the states
to collect revenues required for the investment program's matching grants and recurrent expenditures.
Te project would assist all four states to: (a) complete updating their cadastres by the end of
1991; and (b) reassess property values by the end of 1992. Several studies have been initiated to
codify and modernize their fiscal systems; a major study on fiscal laws and state budgets was
conducted in the course of project preparation. No new taxes are being proposed, but major
shortfalls in the operation and collection of existing taxes and levies have been identified. A
commission to review state fiscal laws and draft proposals will be established by June 1991 in each of
the four states. New state riscal codes will be drafted before the end of 1992 and the respective
bills presented to the state legislative branches. The Policy Letter (Annex 1) addresses the aims
regarding these issues, and the action programs agreed with each of the four states would be
incorporated in the Execution Annexes of the CUD Agreement.
3.27         As municipalities increase their institutional capacity, a larger part of the state
revenue-sharing (gartici=acionemw is passed on to them. Under the new Hidalgo Fiscal Coordination
Law at least 20 percent of the participaciones are transferred to the municipalities. When
municipalities are not yet capable of performing a special function as established by law, states retain
this function and charge municipalities for administrative services. It is estimated that, due to
decentralization, transfers to municipalities will increase by about 15 percent (Chiapas) to 100 percent
(Hidalgo) in real terms.



-27 -
3.28          Reform of the formulas for allocating revenue-shasing passed on by the state to
municipalities will be promoted during the project. In some states the formula is unclear; in others it,
poses problems of inefficiency and inequity. A reform is impotant since almost 80 percent of the -
revenue of municipalities in these states derives from those transfers. Hidalgo has taken the lead and
enacted a law containing a new formula based on population, local tax effort, and a marginality index,
in close agreement with Bank proposals. The other three states have initiated studies required for
moderniztion of their revenue-sharing formulas. It is expected that new draft laws would be
compieled by June 1991 and presented to state congresses in the second half of that year. This
action would also be reflected in the action programs of the project states as referred to in para. 3.11.
Lack of sufficient progress under the monitorable actions in this paragraph and para. 3.26, cggld
constitute a rean for suspending disbursements to a particular state. In such cases, funds could be
canceled or reallocated to other states.
(d) Benefrciary Contributions to Investment Cost
3.29          The CUD Operational Manual incorporates rules for beneficiary contribution.
Beneficiary contributions to investment costs are regulated through the CUD/PNS Manual Unico de
Operacion, table "Apertura Programatica y Estructura Financiera." Productive projects are financed
through a mix of grants (from 75 percent to a poor state down to 50 percent to a well-off state) and
loans (from 50 percent in a well-off state down to 25 percent in a poor state) that have to be secured
by the beneficiaries, prior to project execution. Part of the grant is federal (maximum of 50 percent
to a poor state), and the rest is a matching grant from the state. States also require municipal and/or
beneficiary contributions for such that are incorporated in the state matching grant. In a survey
carried out of subprojects during 1989, cash contributions represented from 1 to 9 percent of project
costs and materials, and community labor (provided free) from 10 to 15 percent of project costs (see
Annex 5). Social projects are financed by grants from the federal government (from 75 percent in a
poor state down to 50 percent in a well-off state) and the rest has to be covered by a state matching
grant. States also request municipal and/or beneficiary contributions. To build a prinury school or a
rural road, the beneficiaries are required to contribute local materials and construction work (as free
community work-1i2). These contributions represent 20 percent to 30 percent of the cost of the
project.u
3. Flnancing Recurrent and Operation and Maintenance Costs
3.30          For each sectoral investment program financed in a state under the CUD, the sectoral
committee of the COPLADE will provide a financing plan for recurrent costs. For each subproject
to be financed there must be a plan for financing recurrent and operation and mantnce (O&M)
costs, as part of the project file to be submitted to the COPLADE. Such a rule is included in the
CUD/PNS Operational Manual and applied to productive, water supply, and education sectors. For
example, the project will not fuiance construction of a new school unless provision has been made for
paing -its teachers. Such budgetary agreements and O&M plans wil be reviewed during project
supevision, as part of the annual reviews.
3.31          The estimate for major recurrent costs in Annex 8 shows that incremental recurrent
costs will amount to about US$7 million equivalent in 1991 and will rise by the end of the project to
about US$36 million equivalent. Maintenance of rural and feeder roads is accorded top priority in

the CUD Operating Manual. Maintenance of feder roads built or rehabilitated under the project will

3.32         For operation and maintenance, the community or group of beneficiaries would sign
an agreement with the executing agency under which the beneficiaries accept an obligation to fully
recover operation and maintenance costs. This agreement between the executing agency and the
community would be part of the "Acta de Aceptacion por la Comunidad' that is now included in
every subproject request to the COPLADES (expediente tecnico). An amendment in the Guia para Ia
Elaboracion de Expedientes Tecnicos Simplificados, in that regard, has been introduced. Institutional
arrangements for operation and maintenance proposed under the Water Supply and Sanitation Sector
Loan would be extended to this project (Annex 6). Pricing and tariff policies for water supply and
irrigation are addressed under these respective sectoral loans. In electrification projects, the Federal
Commission for Electricity (CFE) has clear and reasonable guidelines that cover capital costs and
operation and maintenance. It requires a 50 percent previous deposit, of capital costs, by the
beneficiaries before it starts any electrification project. In the case of rural roads built under PNS,
after the road is built, it is formally transferred to the municipality and an agreement is signed
between the executing agency, SPP, and the municipality under which the executing agency will
provide technical support. SPP will provide resources under the FMS and the municipality will take
responsibility for maintaining the road (para. 3.49 and Annex 6).
4. Coordination with Sectoral Policies
3.33         Coordination between the proposed loan and the sectoral-specific loans is assured at
the state level through the CUD mechanism. First, the CUD Operational Manual will enforce the
same cost-recovery rules and project design and efficiency criteria consistent with Bank sector-specific
loans. Second, through the POAs and eligibility criteria defined in the CUD Operational Manual the
assignment of projects to the existing Bank loans would be coordinated. This project therefore
defines no separate sector strategies or rules. The following paragraphs, however, describe sectoral
priorities for the poor in the four states and the relevant sectoral rules. While SPP and state planning
agencies coordinate state investment plans and monitor execution through the COPLADE, sectoral
subcommittees coordinate sectoral investnents, and federal or state line agencies and municipalities
execute them. Technical and operation rules are the responsibility of the federal technical ministries.

(a) Agialtu
3.34          The four project states have important agricultural potential. To develop this
potential, public sector investment in infrastructure, technical assistance, technological development,
and research is vital. While the Government continues providing limited credit for agriculture, the
supply of inputs and marketing are increasingly the responsibility of the private sector. The basic
strategy being pursued by the Government with Bank assistance in the four states is to: (a) concentrate
most agricultural services in regions with agroecological potential; (b) develop conditions for market-
oriented agriculture based on the comparative advantages of these regions for growing export products
(tropical fruits and off-season vegetables) and for supplying the domestic market (arge urban centers);
and (c) improve productivity in subsistence agriculture by using simple technologies for moisture
retention and soil conservation, appropriate seeds, and fertilizers.
3.35          For the poorest regions of the states, largely exploited by e}idos and other community-
based organizations, the Government has been providing grants for development of irrigation
infrastructure, livestock, forestation and some agroindustries. In the past, these projects have shown
varying rates of success, depending on many factors ranging from low-grade soils and poor climate to
lack of marketing initiatives.
3.36          The investment programs of 1990-93, for the four states, to be financed by the Bank,
amnount to about US$327 million for agriculture, livestock, and forestry. Of this total, about US$153
million is for primary production and productivity development; US$133 million for small irrigation;
and US$29 million for livestock.'s The Bank would finance the following categories of subprojects
of the CUD manual:2' agricultural promotion and extension including forestation, plant nursery, soil
conservation, watershed protection, drainage, horticulture, animal husbandry, plant and animal disease
control, and coffee plantation rehabilitation (category 3F); construction and rehabilitation of small
irrigation schemes (EA); and agroindustrial production and processing of animal products, fruits and
vegetables, and fishing products (FD).
3.37          In Oaxaca and Hidalgo, priority under the proposed project is given to small irrigation
projects worth about US$69 and US$48 million, respectively. Programs for Guerrero and Chiapas
call for no more than US$10 million each for the four-year program. In the primary production
subcomponent, almost US$50 million is allocated to coffee growers to increase productivity and allow
further vertical integration and crop diversification. Guerrero and Hidalgo are particularly active in
the livestock subsector. The latter state has extensive experience supplying Mexico City with meat,
eggs and poultry; the former relies substantially on this type of project to alleviate poverty among
rural groups. All four states have forestry projects, widt Oaxaca according them the most importance
and Chiapas the least.
3.38          The following types of subprojects ar, emphasized in the proposed project: small-
scale forestation, nurseries, soil conservation, watershed studies and protection, plant and animal
disease control, zero tillage efforts, small drainage, minall-scale horticulture, and small husbandry.
The projects are being developed on a community-based framework with proper technologies and
technical assistance.

3.39         Agriculture potential and ecological and climatic conditions are extremely diverse in
the four states. Thus, technologies for improving productivity must be developod locally, with on-

decentralization of iostitutional activity is being pursued forcefuly. The SARH has begun
transferring resources and functions to rural development districts, and more than 100 agreements
have been signed between SARH and producer groups, giving the latter resources to hire technical
assistance directly.
3.44         The Bank is now financing an agricultural extension project (PROCATI) under Loan
2859-ME. Beginning in 1987, with Bank financing of US$20 million and a project cost of USS13.8
million, and to be completed on December 31, 1991, PROCATI aims to improve the efficiency of
extension services in 25 rural development districts (DDR) and to raise the capacity of SARH to

small farmers in the four states with the same approach used in PROCATI. This will be done by

provided assurances during negotiations that an estimated US$3 million will be allocated to SARH in
1992 for the proposed districts. In 1993 an equivalent amount would be allocated to SARH, possibly
under the proposed Agricultural Technology Loan, under preparation.
(b) Social Services
3.41         TMe main social sector component financed by the project is education. A parallel
project (Basic Health Care Loan) hac been recently approved by the Board. The Bank is also
preparing with the Government a Primary Education Loan, possibly starting in 1992, to finance
investments in the four project states. Notwithstanding this, some urgent action is needed in the
education subsector, particularly in rehabilitating and completing some long delayed infrastructure.
All the above projects cover the four poorest states.
3.42         The education sectoral programs for the four states were designed based on the
Government's Educational Modernization Program (PME) for 1989-94 which is defined at the
national level and in the state sector diagnosis. PME stresses developing primary education in
marginal areas and extending it to all populations; completing the education cycle for schools that lack
six years of primary education; and providing specific education programs for indigenous
communities. Consequently, the strategy is to: (a) reduce the gap in the coverage of primary
education in the four states by two-thirds by the end of the program; (b) upgrade the quality of social
services statewide; (c) develop and expand secondary education to respond to increased demand; and
(d) expand the educational models to foster and preserve the indigenous cultures.'
3.43         The Government's PME targets for primary education in the four states are to: (a)
bring terminal efficiency up to the national average (about 55 percent); (b) extend the number of
grades in at least 80 percent of the schools; (c) decrease the drop-out rate by at least 20 percent; and
(d) reduce to one-third the rate of failures. In addition, in Chiapas, it would seek to reduce the
number of multi-grade schools by eight percent.

(c) State and Rural Roads
3.48         Between 40 and 50 percent of the population of the four states lack regular road
communication, using mostly dirt tracks with irreguiar accessibility. An additional 17,000 kn of
roads are needed, based on the agricultural potential and social needs of communities with more than
500 inhabitants, which amounts to about 40 percent of the national gap according to estimates of
SCT. The minimum standard used by SCT is that all communities of more than 200 people should
have a road link, up to a reasonable cost. From 1973 to 1983, about 1,500 kn of new roads were
built each year. With financial retrenchment, that rate has fallen to about 100 km in the 1986-88
period. In addition, funds for maintenance and rehabilitation have been sharply reduced. A recent
assessment of the Oaxaca road network showed that only 15 percent was in good condition while 40
percent could only be of service if totally rebuilt. All four states sustained substantial road damage
from tropical storms in the last two years.
3.49         The investment program to be partly financed would: (a) extend the routine
maintnance program to about 70 percent of the rural road network; (b) permit major rehabilitation
and reconstruction of the rural road network in order te increase the share of roads in 'good
condition' from 21 to 77 percent of a projected 22,000 km network; and (c) provide feeder and rural
road links for the first time to about one mrilion people, once the construction of about 5,000 km of
new feeder and rural roads is finished. Those roads are needed to foster agricultural potential of these
regions and to link communities with at least 500 inhabitants with the health and education systems.
About US$107 million would be spent on rehabilitation of rural roads; US$194 million on



- 32 -
cviLstructitn of new rural roads; and US$9 mnillion on maintenance of these riads. For state feeder
roads, about US$5 miton would be spent on rehabilitation; US$81 milion on upgrading; and US$3
million on maintenance. Priority will lie given to completing roads already under cotstruction M..e
total progran would benefit about 3.6 million people. It represents an ambitious program, even by
pre-crisis standards' (about 1,250 km of new roads built each year). By the end of the four-year
program, an estimated 30 percent of the road gap would be elimnated. The project would finance
subprojects in category EK of the lC slD corresponiding to construction, renovation, and rehabilitation
of state, rural, and feeder roads and bridges, and maintenance of such roads.
3.50         Mexico has the capacity to implement the pioposed road program. Virtually all
project preparation is being subcontfacted to consulting engineering firms, and about 80 percent of the
projects are being executed by the private sector thr(ough local competitive bidding. The program of
rural roads implemented in 1989 already approaches 900 km. Guerrero, Oaxaca, and Hidalgo have
also acquired a significant stock of road equipment, which is being utilized to establish subregional
equipment centers for rehabilitation and major maintenance work. Some municipalities (Hidalgo) and
communities (Oaxaca) have participated in rural road construction. The result has been a significant
increase in implementation capabilities and in the share of costs borne by the communities. During
the project, such experiences would be consolidated and evaluated.
3.51         In Chiapas, the program is being executed by two state agencies: the Comision Estatal
de Caminos and the Secr_tariat of Urban Development and Communication (SDUC) in equal shares.
In Guerrero the state agency MAQUlNOP manages road equipment depots and incorporates the Junta
Estatal de Caminos. In Hidalgo most of the program is being carried out by the state Junta de
Caminos. In Oaxaca the program is being executed by SCT with increasing participation from the
state agency (Caminos y Autopistas de Oaxaca - CAO). In all states the construction program is
closely supervised by SCT, through CONACAL, the recently formed fideicomiso in charge of feeder
and rural roads.
3.52         The eligibility grid for Bank financing of roads is presented in Annex 4,
Attachment 2, to be incorporated in the CUD Operational Manual. For rural roads (caminos rurales)
the economic and social evaluation are basically based on the cost per persoin served that should not
exceed US$500. Whei.. the cost is greater than US$500, a full economic analysis would be carried
out and present an economic rate of return (ERR) higher than 12 percent. The US$500 threshold is
based on economic analysis of past projects v:ider similar conditions. In addition, only construction
of type E or F (roads/track) is eligible. For the construction of new feeder roads (carreteras
alimentadotas), a full economic evaluation generally based on transportation cost savings would reveal
an ERR not less than 12 percent. Paving works would not be eligible. For routine maintenance,
rehabilitation, and spot improvement, an economic evaluation would not be required for rural roads.
For feeder roads, the rehabilitation subproject, including spot improvement, would be eligible only
for unpaved roads. A threshold of US$15,000/km would be established. Under the threshold no
economic evaluation would be required; over the threshold, an economic evaluation would be
required showing an ERR not less than 12 percent. In order to coordinate with other Bank projects,
only types F and E roads (rural roads), following SCT terminology, would be accepted for financing,
plus unpaved D and C categories (feeder roads).
3.53         State agencies maintain feeder roads. SCT closely monitors budgets and
implementation capacity. Under current institutional arrangements, SCT is responsible for
maintaining rural roads, but maintenance has been poor due to lack of resources and low priority. In

(d) R-tural Inrrtr
3.54          Shortages in water supply, sewerage, electricity, housing, and other basic
infrastructure arc widespread in the four project states. Although the Government recognizes such
needs, some priorities have been established. Such priorities depend to a large extent on benefit/cost
elements and are revealed by social demand through the participatory planning process.
3.55          Shortages of safe water are more serious in Oaxaca and Hidalgo, and Hidalgo is also
seriously affected by pollution from the discharge of Mexico City sewers. About 60 percent of
Qaxaca's population lacks piped water. In Chiapas the proportion is about 40 percent, but with large
regional variation. Only 23 percent of Guerrero's population lacks piped water, but of this group
two-thirds depend upon very poor quality water. Poor administration and lack of adequate water
treatment are among the main causes of the poor quality of water services.
3.56          The investment program to be financed by the Bank for water and sewerage totals
about US$127 million and benefits a population of about 2 million. Most of the investment will occur
in localities with 500 to 10,000 inhabitants. The Bank will finance CUD categories corre.sponding to
construction and rehabilitation of water supply, drainage, and sewerage systems and water treatment
plants (EC); and extension of water supply, drainage, and sewerage systems (ED). The Gzvi-ri'nent
agreed to cause the CNA to provide technical support as required to the state water agencies in the
preparation of such subprojects and in the development of institutional strengthening programs.
3.57          Systems of water supply and sewerage would be built only if the community
(municipality) establishes satisfactory arrangements for collecting user fees from beneficiaries to cover
operation and maintenance (Annex 5). Operation of water supply and sewerage systems will be
subject to guidelines established in Annex 6 which have been included in the CUD Operational
Manual.
3.58          Mexico has made an enormous effort to extend electrical service to rural areas.
About 72 percent of the rural population now has access to service. However, according to the CFE,
about 556,000 people in Guerrero lack electricity (39 percent of the state's rural population), 537,000
in Chiapas (34 percent), and 241,000 in Hidalgo (23 percent). Oaxaca has already been able to
reduce significantly the number of people without electricity, despite the large gap at the beginning of
the last decade.
3.59          The investment program for rural electricity for 1990-93 amounts to US$91 million,
benefiting an estimated I million persons. Oaxaca and Guerrero requested about US$33 million each,
Hidalgo US$21 million, and Chiapas US$4 million. The Bank will finance category EU of the CUD

(e} EtUngJL the Envi=anzn
3,61         lTe four states include some of the most delicate environmental areas, including the
last remaining tropical rain forests in Mexico. The project therefore includes specific measures for
ensuring more balanced development and for protecting critical reserves.
3.62          The Lacandona Reserve is part of the second largest tropical humid forest on the
North American continent. The Lacandona covers about I million ha and has a population of about
70,000, mostly Indians. It is extremely rich in rare wood species, animals, and cultural sites.
According to data from the Forestry Subsecretariat of SARH, from 1969 through 1982 about 40
percent of the forest was destroyed and 14 percent of the biosphere was lost due to wood extraction,
slash-and-burn agriculture, and excessive grazing (Landsat of May 1988). The Lacandona Reserve
includes: (a) the Montes Azules biosphere zone, designated by President Salinas as a human
patrimony under the UNESCO pro6ram, which is also referred to as the core; and (b) the buffer zone
constituted by five subregions that surround the biosphere zone. The area has also been subject to
population pressure caused by conflicts in neighboring Guatemala.
3.63          Significant progress has aleady been made in consving the Lacandona Reserve.
Logging has been prohibited in the zone and has effectively come to a halt last year. The area
affected by slash-and-burn fires has decreased by about 80 percent In 1989, relative to the
previous year. The state government has created a special conmittee within COPLADES to
coordinate all institutions working in the are. The Lacandona conservation program, drawn-up by
the state and cleared by SEDUE, defines an integrated strategy for the biosphere zone and buffer
areas for the next five years. An institutional framework has been designed with multisector
subprojects, with a horizon of about 10 years. The total cost of this subcomponent is about US$40
million for the 1990-93 period and would be pardy financed by the present project. The main
element of the plan forbids any logging in the biosphere zone of Montes Azules (331.000 ha) and
includes strict regulations and policing of the reserve to protect it from additional human
encroachment.' The Government is already undertaking a major effort to resolve land conflicts in
the reserve, and halt land titling, and prohibit road construction in the core zone. To decrease
pressure on the biosphere area, productive and social actions would be undertaken in buffer zones to
provide alternatives to resource exploitation (e.g., reforestation, using rubber and coffee plantations;
incentives for using areas with high agricultural potential more effectively; and disincentives to such
damaging activities as livestock grazing and slash-and-burn agriculture). Finally, regulations to
discourage migration into the buffer zone would be implemented (e.g., severe limitations on land
titling for new migrants and on human settlements). The Wlan. d%-,ibed in greater detail in Amiex 9.

implementation on the agreed plans would constitute sufficient reason to suspend disbursements to the
state of Chiapas. In that case, funds could be reallocated to other states.
(f) Protection and Rehabilitation of Cultural Patrimony
3.64         The four project states include some of Mexico's richest and most famous pre-
Colombian archeological sites. To protect these sites, the National Institute of Anthropology and
History (1NAH) has designed a systematic cultural property component as part of the project. The
cultural property component will use local labor and materials to restore 10 pre-Colombian
archeological sites and one colonial-era convent (the Augustine convent of Actopan in the state of
Hidalgo). The archeological sites to be restored under the project include the classical Mayan ruins
of Palenque in Chiapas, the still little-known but monumental Xochipala site in Guerrero, the Toltec
city of Tula in Hidalgo, and the famous Zapotec ceremonial center of Monte Alban in the Valley of
Oaxaca. The subcomponent would also support INAH and INI in the establishment of a state
inventory of archeological sites in Chiapas and Oaxaca.
3.65         A major aspect of the cultural property component will be to mobilize local labor and
use local materials for the restoration work, under the professional supervision of the INAH
archeological staff. Thus, of the total budget of US$2.6 million for the cultural property component,
INAH estimates that 85 percent (or US$2.2 million) will benefit local communities in the form of
wages, and 10 percent (or US$0.26 million) wIll be spent for local materials such as lumber, sand,
rock, and cement. The reforestation projects would also produce indirect social and economic
benefits in the form of increased tourist revenues for local communities. Monte Alban, for example,
threatened by neglect and by population pressures on its periphery, receives nearly 300,000 tourists
annually but lacks basic sanitation facilities.

(a) Preiration -of Investment Prrans
3.67          The investment programs were prepared with Bank participation from the very start
using a 'bottom-up" approach with wide popular participation, in the following way:
O At a first seminar in Oaxaca, state officials and federal government representatives
defined the basic strategy, discussed sectoral priorities, and fixed criteria for program
formulation. They also agreed wide democratic participation was required to identify
needs and formulate projects.
O   Between the first and second seminar, state governments carried out detailed regional
studies based on existing surveys and defined subregional strategies for development
and poverty alleviation. State officials met with all municipal councils representing
the beneficiary population (about 70 per state, each representing about 100,000
persons) and asked them to enunciate the most urgent public investment needs for
each community and rank them by priority. These were computerized (in Hidalgo
there were about 20,000 project proposals) and then subjected to an initial screening
based on guidelines approved by the Bank and SPP.
o   Next, project packages were compared with sectoral/regional strategies and studied
from a technical perspective (technical feasibility and quick economic and social
analysis). Modular projects (like schools or health centers) were screened with the
same technical rules to minimize costs.
O   At a third seminar, in Hidalgo, a budget ceiling was given to each state and
allocation rules were agreed among regions within the states. Between the third and
the fourth seminar, an iterative process took place among municipalities, states, and
the SPP to choose a final investment program. Line and technical agencies and
ministries played an active part to assure the efficiency of the basic program. Simple
new criteria for analyzing investment programs were introduced: sectoral
composition, sectoral strategy compatibility, investment per person, and investment
per poor person per region (the last two indicators permitted monitoring the poverty
orientation of the program).
(b) Proposed Investment Programs
3.68          The total investment programs in the productive sectors (agriculture, forestry, and
fishing only), social sectors, and physical infrastructure average about US$620 million per year for
the four-year program, covering 1990 to 1993, and add up to about US$1,860 million'; (Mex$5,023
million). The result would be to increase investment in the four states over recent levels by about
20 percent in real terms in 1990 relative to 1989, and about 10 percent in real terms annually in
the following 3 years. The program represents a recovery to about 80 percent of the investment
program of the early 1980s. To facilitate disbursement, the Bank only finances the coe progam,

3.70            Infrastructure building and social sector improvements account for almost 75 percent
of planned overall investment (see Table 3.3). This is understandable in view of (a) the needs
articulated by the communities, (b) the role of the public sector, (c) the large lags in infrastructure
building, and (d) the remoteness of most communities. The proposed road infrastructure has the
double appeal of giving communities access to social services (linking the community to the health
system or allowing school teachers to commute to villages) and permitting farmers to transport
surplus products to local mariets.

(c) Ipnu on Cat
3.73          A description of project management and organization is provided in section D, and
Annex l1. No new institutions would be created to implement the state investment plans.
Coordination would be carried out by SPP and the state planning secreariats, under the CUD
mechanism, one of the more sophisticated regional planning systems with the longest experience in
Latin America. Through this system, that started with the PIDERs and was followed by the PDRs of
the last Administration, investments even larger than those proposed have been implemented in the
early 1980s, before the fiscal retrenchment. Through the introduction of state COPLADEs, the
Operation Manual, and a Monitoring and Evaluation System, the regional planning system has clearly
reached maturity. Nevertheless, under the institutional development component discussed below, and
highlighted in Annex 1, Attachment 2, the project would continue to improve CUD medcanisms
(para. 3.79); strengthen Commissions for Municipal Development Planning (COPLADEMs) at the
municipal level (para. 3.83); improve project appraisal and selection (para. 3.17); improve the rules
for revenue sharing (paras. 3.26 and 3.28); introduce a quantum leap in monitoring and evaluation
(para. 3.94); and add environmental criteria (vara. 3.23).

3.74         As detailed in Annex 10, an analysis of execution capacity exists for building the
proposed infrastructure. Federal and state agencies were largely underfunded due to the fiscal
retrenchment in the investment program that, since 1983, has been cut by over 60 percent in most of
the sectors to be financed. The analysis of the 1990 investment program showed that about 8Q
percent of the construction activity was carried out by contracting to the private sector. Road
construction investment, for example, almost doubled in 1989, and last year the program represented
an increase of about one third, with dozens of private construction firms engaged in the program.
Almost all project preparation and technical feasibility studies would also be subcontracted to
consulting firms. There is no guarantee, of course, that implementation capacity is adequate for the
increased responsibilities that lower-level jurisdictions would have to assume. Therefore, plans for
hiring additional staff, mainly professional and engineers, have been formulated and started to be
implemented (see section 6 below).
3.75         Under the present CUD rules, if there is a serious slippage in the execution of an
agreed investment program, the state is penalized by reducing the floor for negotiating the following
year's investment programn to the amount that was implemented. In deciding on the levels of the
investment program, SPP and the Bank, would take in consideration the demonstrated institutional
capacity and the proposed institutional development program agreed with the state, including the
expansion of technical and engineering personnel required for an efficient implementation of the
investment subprojects.
S. PoectSupgorted Institutional PDevlogmmt
3.76         The project-supported Institutional Development Component aims at strengthening
existing organizational structures. The only new units created, the federal and state Special
Coordinating Groups, will become part of the permanent institutional structure. Extensive
institutional analysis was carried out of key federal and state agencies as well as of all
municipalities. Federal and four state groups were set for identifying institutional capacity gaps,
given the objectives of the project. Those groups reviewed interinstitutional relationships, and
analyzed, task by task, all functions currently assigned to each agency, and estimated what each
agency would require if decentralization were carried out. Municipalities within each state were
surveyed to define their stage of development.3 A detailed Institutional development program
was drawn up and cleared by SPP for each state and fedeal agency that would be involved in
project implementation during 1990-93 (para. 3.81). Institutional development in the health sector
would be financed and monitored through the Basic Health Care Project, in the water and sewerage
sector under the Water Supply and Sanitation Sector Loan; in agriculture it would be monitored
through PROCATI; and in education through the Basic Education Project, after it is approved. The
present project coordinates those action programs by fimancing institutional development programs for
subproject preparation, appraisal, and design across sectors and for state and regional planning. It
also supports institutional development in the road and environment sectors. Detailed annual
operation programs will be drawn up, specifying actions, training, and technical assistance. These
programs will be reviewed by the Bank during the annual reviews and will be included as annexes to
the POAs. Prior to effectiveness, consultants with qualifications acceptable to the Bank will be hired,
under agreed terms of reference, to assist in the management of the institutional development
programs, and the first year institutional development program will be presented to the Bank. No

Deeentralization of Public Expenditure Control
3.77          In 1990 the Government increased its allocation to regional investment programs by
about 17 percent nationwide, in real terms, compared to 1989. This included an increase in the CUD
budget of about 12 percent plus a transfer of investment resources from the federal line ministries to
regional programs under state control. The Policy Letter (Annex 1) summarizes the Government
actions proposed on: (a) decentralization in agriculture and roads described in the Decentralization
Matrix; and (b) state action programs for fiscal modernization and resource mobilization as well as
for improving equity in distribution of revenue-sharing among municipalities. The Banlk would have
the right to suspend disbursements if these programs are not satisfactorily implemented. In addition,
the following commitments of the Mexican Government are part of the Policy Letter:
(a)    a substantial increase in the investment for poverty alleviation. The federal
contribution under the CUD for each of the four states will be increased by at least
five percent in real terms per year during the 1990-1993 period over the federal
contribution given in 1989. By the end of FY93 the federal contribution under the
CUD for the four states combined would be at least 50 percent above the 1989 level,
in real terms;
(b)    a considerable transfer (nationwide) of investments controlled by federal line agencies
under the Programa Normal Federal to the CUD, over the 1991-93 period, carried out
through the PNS, that in 1991 was allocated 15 percent of total public investment; and
(c)    an increase in the federal allocation to the municipal funds (FMS) by a minimum of
5 percent a year, in real terms, during 1991, 1992 and 1993, in the four project
statesNs.
3.78          Nationwide, current and capital expenditures associated with the activities to be
decentralized account for about 10 percent of federal expenditures. These will be gradually
transferred to the states, implying a decrease in federal control from 80 percent to 70 percent, with a
yearly decrease of 3.3 percent in the 1990-93 period. To effect such transfers, functions being
decentralized will be costed state by state, and the amounts will be transferred to the states from the
respective sectoral ministries through the federal budget. Ihe annual operational institutional
development programs will specify the actions required in the gradual decentralization process.
(a) Strengthening State Institutions
3.79          The program for improving state administration involves: (a) reinforcing the state
capabilities for strategic planning, project evaluation, and resource management; (b) improving the
system of budgeting, controlling, and auditing public accounts; (c) increasing the capacity for
technical assistance to municipalities; (d) improving technical assistance for agriculture and rural

3.80         Interinstitigonl relatignshis have been defined clearly at the different levels of
government and among all involved federal and state agencies as well as municipalities. Particular
attention has been paid to the elimination of parallel structures. Functions analyzed include planning,
budgeting, execution, and monitoring. Disaggregation of those roles into more precise functions and
tasks took place. A committee In each state will supervise the administrative reform during the
r.ext four years. That committee will make recommenaations to the state governments at the
end of each year for possible readjustments and fine-tuning.
3.81         Institutional capacity gps were identified for every implementing agency or
administrative unit with some project involvement in terms of projet functons and tasks, including
deficiencies in legislation and interinstitutional and internal organizational problems. The project
would help to bridge or mitigate these gaps through the following subcomponents (detailed cost tables
are in Annex 10, Attachment 1):
(a)    Tecnical Assistance, including (i) 22 staff months of organization and financial
management specialists for the central level and as many more for each of the four
states, for a total of 110 staff months; (ii) 50 person-months for each state road
institution, for a total of 200 staff-months; and (iii) assistance in the development of
an updated SCT manual for the feeder roads. This assistance would help install
modem project management procedures and review norms, inter-institutional
relationships, and internal distribution of functions so as to establish, as early as
possible, a smooth flow of management functions at the federal level and between the
federal, state, and municipal levels. Thereafter, they would help launch and deliver
training activities accordingly. The total cost of this subcomponent is US$5.9 million.
(b)    Skill Uperading Activities, including over 200 training activities, informative
meetings, courses, seminars, fellowships, and others. These training activities would
help bridge gaps in (i) management skills such as overall project management and
coordination functions; (ii) professional skills such as planning, programming and
budgeting, public finance control (auditing), and supervision activities; (iii) technical
skills such as systems operation, monitoring and evaluation, budgeting, and project
appraisal; and (iv) engineering design and supervision. Total cost of this
subcomponent is estimated at US$5.5 million.
(c)    Equipment, providing for purchase of office equipment, furniture, and technical
equipment; and printing information and diffusion material. The cost of this
subcomponent is included in the state investment programs.
(d)    Technical Assistance and other support for the operation of the Indian Development
Funds. Total cost of this subcomponent is estimated at US$3.2 million.

improve the knowledge of social conditions in these states. Topics to be studied are: (a) project
identification in the four states; (b) rural employment and specific needs of poor migrant households,

(b)    gthuing MunicigaiLi
3.83         Municipalities in the four states are at extremely varied stages of development. While
some municipalities in major cities already enjoy relatively sophisticated administration, others are not
able to perform simple administrative functions. A typology of municipalities developed within the
project groups municipalities into four stages of development. For each group a development strategy
has been formulated in terms of instinal development, training, and technical assistance.
Subregional offices, coordinated by COPLADEs, have been set up to provide technical assistance to
municipalities. Increasingly, funds are channeled from states to municipalities through Development
Agreements between States and Municipalities (CUDEMUNs), with federal participation, that are part
of the annual negotiated programs.
(c) Shrnthening lmtitutions to Implement Environm_ntal PoliHde
3.84         Mexico has an adequate legal basis for environmental protection, but it lacks the
institutional capability to enforce the law. The environmental objectives of the project are to:
(a) ensure that the investment program pursued in the project states does not cause further degradation
of the enviretiment; (b) support the protection of natural reserve areas, particularly the Lacandona
Reserve, th last tropical humid forest in Mexico; and (c) strengthen state institutions that work on
environm  Wal issues. Annex 9 provides an analysis of the environmental component.
3.85         The project would seek to strengthen environmental management instruments in the
four states by providing additional higher- and medium-level staff, training, operational support and
equipment, improvements in subproject assessment, support for drafting state and municipal
environmental legislation and regulations, agroecological zoning, and other support activities as
needed. It will support SEDUE state offices as well as environmental services to be provided by state
and municipal agencies.
3.86         The project would help to pro   natural areas in the four states by upgrading patrol
services, preparing management plans, consolidating land tenure as needed, organizing community
action programs in buffer zones, and other measures. It will give priority to protection services for
the most threatened sites.
3.87         The institutional development component described in sections (a) to (c) would be
monitored through the performance and progress indicators in Annex 1, Attachment 2.
D. Project Organization and Managent
3.88         Structure and Functions. SPP, as the executing agency, would have primary
responsibility for the project. The PNS Unit within SPP coordinates the national program. PNS
finances projects for poverty alleviation nationwide according to evaluation criteria similar to those
used in the four states. The PNS, at the federal level, would have a Special Coordinating Group
(SCG) with a core staff assigned to support the project in the four disadvantaged states. Similar

include staff from budgeting and pro g, financial ad an, n n  and evaluation,
and technical support. The functions of the SCGs and a project organIzational chart are provided in
Annex 11.
3.89         SPP at federal level would coordinate implementation of the project through the CUD
system, with state and federal line agencies working in the states providing the actions and technical
assistance required. Ile baic oeratinal instpment to be uSed for proxect ilmlmentation would be

3.90         State governments, through the planning secretariats, are responsible for defining and

3.91         Decentalization is coordinated by SPP as part of its responsibility for formulating

3.92         Project implementation and supervision would be enhanced through seminars,
organized at state level, where representatives from the four states, the federal level, and the Bank
would be present. Such seminars would take place twice a year, rotate among the states, and cover
preparation of state investment programs, comparison of experiences, ad evaluation of progress
achieved under each project.
3.93         Fund Flow Progeutes (Annex 11, Attachments 2 and 3). Once SPP authorizes a
state's annual plans and agrees on financing, it requests the Treasury to open a revolving account for
each state at a correspondent bank through Banco de Mexico. NAFIN, with Bank funds, opens a
special account at the Central Bank. After agencies execute their projects, they prepare SOEs and
submit them for payment at the designated bank. The SPP regional delegate office prepares summary
sheets of expenditures which are first aggregated at the state level, and overall, subsequently, at the
project level to be submitted as summary SOEs by NARIN for Bank disbursement, under the Special
Account.
1. Projeet Monitoring and Ezaluaion ME)
3.94         The project would introduce rigorous and systematic monitoring and evaluation
(M&E) methods including an improved data collection system and expanded electronic capacity
(Annex 12). Procedures have already been adopted to allow tracing of subproject costs and execution



-44 -
over a umMber of years. Additional monitorable variables,X3 besides those related to financial and
phyical progress, would be intrduced to address the performnce and achievemen of subproject
objectives, completion status, ex-post utilization of works or outputs, and status of participation in
cost recovery and operations and maintenance. Extensive improvements are expected, for example,
in more appropriate and timely feedback, in project implementation hough the use of simplified
formats, and in more efficient task distribution between federal and state levels. A draft manual
outlining definitions, functions, and procedures proposed for federal, state, and project levels for
M&E has been agreed with the Bank and formally approved. The unit to carry out those functions has
also been appropriately staffed. That the Monitoring and Evaluation System be established and
operational would be a condition for effectiyness.
3.95         Macro-indicators to measure overall project progress and performance are included
in Annex 1, Attachment 2. For the subcomponents involving indigenous areas and environment,
separate indicators are being prepared. For the investment program, investment expenditures per
capita and per poor, by regions, will be used as the main indicators of progress and performance.
State poverty ind--es as well as executing agency and NGO monitoring databases would provide
baseline and supplementary information for evaluation. Additional information would be provided by
introduction of the proposed living standard minimum survey.
3.96         Monitoring the progress of the project at the state level would be the responsibility of
the M&E section of the state SCG and quarterly progress reports would be submitted to the federal
SCG Unit. The federal SCO Unit would then consolidate these reports and submit an annual project
progress repoft to the Bank no later than the end of the second quarter of each year, which would
incorporate the recommendations of the annual project reviews. The format, as agreed with the
Bank, would reflect the information provided through the above M&E system.
3.97         The M&E system is being improved by cross-fertilization among states and with Bank
assistance. The system to be introduced under the proposed project would make it possible to
monitor improvement in the quality of public services, especially in the area of institutional reform.
The system would permit classification of projects by agency and thus clarify agencies'
responsibilities in project activity. It would also guide impact analysis at the community level and
help in formulating regional and sectoral policies. The use of the new appraisal techniques will
improve routine monitoring and provide more precise information on causes for shortfalls that may
arise. The application of expert systems for certain M&E functions would be developed. The
Government agreed that an annual evaluation of the project would be carried out by the federal M&E
Unit by January 31 of each year to provide conclusions and recommendations simultaneously with the
SP/Bank annual project review in the first quarter of each year.

capable of carrying out this type of project work. Many social community investments would be
carried out by the beneficiaries themselves under written agreements (convenios) with the
municipalities, through the FMS or federal supervizory line agencies. This would be the case for the
many small civil works and maiatenance projects carried out by communities, ejidos, small producer
organizations, and NGOs under the FMS, where quantities of works cannot be well defined in
advance and where works are small and scattered in remote areas with no local contractors interest
or available. In the case of the many small community subprojecs carried out by the FMS,
communities are required to provide free unskilled labor and local materials. Since those works are
scattered throughout more than 800 municipalities, and expected to be very small in size, in many
cases only one or very few suppliers in the area would be able to supply the goods or materials
required. Ihus, in order to recgn1ize the poverty alleviation and community participation efforts,
direct contracting is allowed, but restricted to purchasing of construction materials and small goods
for the FMS.

costing below US$5 million and above US$150,000 and for goods costing less than US$250,000
equivalent and more than US$50,000 would be done through local competitive bidding (LCB), under

4. Audit
3.107          Prior to submiss.on to the Bank, all financial statements and documents, including
SOEs for which reimbursement would be claimed from the Bank loan, would be subject to
independent review and approval by SPP in accordance with its established control procedures.
Records and accounts maintained by Project States would be audited by private firms designated in
accordance with the Bank's current understanding with SECOGEF. Each participating agency,
including the Central Bank and the participating banks, would maintain separate accounts for project
expenditures. These accounts, and the Special Account, would be audited every year by independent
auditors satisfactory to the Bank. During negotiations assurances were provided from the federal and
the state govermnents that: (a) the Special Account and the project accounts would be maintained and
audited annually according to practices satisfactory to the Bank; (b) terms of reference for the auditors
would include, inter alia, audit of the SOEs for the project accounts (NAFIN and SPP); and (c) copies
of all annual audit reports would be submitted to the bank within six months of the close of each
project fiscal year.
3.108           Auditing of SPP and other federal agencies would be framed within the general
agreement that exists between the Bank and Mexico and taking into account as much as possible the
availability of audit skills at the state level. For those projects executed by states and municipalities,
SECOGEF would prepare the terms of reference, stratify the sample of projects to be audited, plan
the audits, and designate acceptable private state audit firms to be agreed before loan effectiveness.
Agreement on these arrangements were reached during negotiations. The terms of reference for such
auditing would have to be agreed with the Bank prior to loan effectiveness. The private audit firms
may count on the cooperation of the technical staff of the State Control Secretariat and would perform
the audits under the coordinatior. of SECOGEF. They would issue individual audit reports for each
state and for Bank purposes accompanied by an adherence opinion by SECOGEF no later than six
months after the close of the fiscal year. The fee for hiring private auditing firms would be charged
to the loan funds, financed under technical assistance.

5. BainSuorsion
3.109        The proposed project would require extensive and close supervision. Besides Bank
normal supervision of all Bank procedures, two seminars will take place, annually. The first
SPP/Bank annual projxct review, to take place in the first quarter of each year, and beginning in
1991, with the participation of the states will assess: (a) progress of the implementation of the
investment program (Annex 1, Attachment 2); (b) the proposed POAs for each of the four states,
using the criteria indicated in para. 3.16, and adjust them to the implementation experience; (d)
progress of the institutional development programs for the four states would be monitored with the
program contained in Annex I, Attachment 2 and developed ir project file documents state by state;
and (d) progress on decentralization and other policy measures agreed in the Policy Letter (Annex 1).
The second seminar will take place by mid-year and will assess implementation issues that have a
commonality to all the states and are of importance for the success of the project. In order to
supervise the large number of civil works, to help the task manager in screening individual projects
that are required to be presented under the loan, encourage and facilitate procurement and
disbursement procedures and help in the implementation process, LAC Country Department II is
planning to station a rural engineer in the Bank's Resident Mission in Mexico.
E. Proiect C:osts
3.110         Total project costs are estimated to be US$1,362.7 million, including US$1,301.6
million for the investment program, and US$61.1 million for technical assistance and conservation of
the environment and cultural patrimony. Annex 7 gives the sectoral and regional composition of the
project components that have been chosen for cost-sharing by the Bank. These estimations have to be
taken as indicative, since revisions could be made in light of implementation capabilities. About 46
percent corresponds to infrastructure projects (roads and power systems), 30 percent to productive
projects (agriculture, forestry and agroindustries), and 24 percent to social projects (education, water
supply, and sewerage systems). These subsectors have been chosen because they are spread over all
regions in the four stes, the poverty orientation of the project and the partition of investment
projects between this project and the Health and Basic Education projects. Oaxaca would account for
about 32 percent of the project with US$431 million, Hidalgo 25 percent (US$334 million), Chiapas
24 percent (US$331 million), and Guerrero 19 percent (US$260 million). The investment program is
composed of about 17,600 subprojects.
3.111        Foreign exchange costs represent about 31 percent of the total project base costs,
representing all direct and indirect costs, and total US$352.7 million. Bank financing of US$350
million would cover about 26 percent of total project costs or 31 percent of the total base cost
CTable 3.7), but equivalent to 78 percent of the foreign exchange costs.

3.112          Costs are based on March 1990 prices and converted at the exchange rate of US$1 to
Mex$2,700 pesos. Costs for civil works and equipments are based on the technical norms of the
sectoral agencies. Fees for technical assistance and other professional services were based on the
Bank's experience with similar locally and internationally recruited experts in the region. Physical
contingencies were computed as 10 percent of the total investment programs, since a large part of the
program is composed of standard projects in which the technical agencies have great experience.
Price contingencies were calculated on a dollar inflation rate of 4.9 percent p.a. and assuming that the
Mexican peso would be adjusted following the purchasing power parity.
F. Benefits and Risks
3.113          Major benefits are expected from (a) improvement in the efficiency of public
administration associated with the effort at furthering and rationalizing decentralization; (b) greater
equity in the financing and provision of public goods to various population groups, sustained by a
permanent change in the formulas for resource allocation; (c) increased capability of the states and
municipalities to carry out development efforts on behalf of the poorest populations; and henceforth
(d) improvement of the welfare of the marginal groups.
3.114          The state programs have been designed clearly under the basic orientation of PNS as
poverty alleviation programs. Since the percentage of people below the absolute poverty line in the
four states is above 50, and rural poverty in most regions exceeds 80 percent, most investments in
rural infrastructure and social sectors will clearly benefit the poor. Moreover, public works under the
proiect would generate an estimated 230.000 man-years of employment in the fur states.

3.115          It is difficult to measure the number of beneficiaries without double counting.
Although the projects have identified the number of beneficiaries-this type of information was
collected for the first time during project preparation-the same community may benefit from a
school, a road, and/or a small irrigation project. With that in mind, Table 3.8 refers mainly to
projects with a local impact. Statewide or regional projects, such as agricultural technical assistance
or an animal sanitary project, have been exduded. Table 3.8 shows that the rural infrastructure
projects, where most investment is concentrated, have a wider impact and would benefit from 1
million to 1.5 million people in each state (lowest number in Guerrero, largest in Chiapas). The
number of beneficiaries from productive projects ranges from about 150,000 in Chiapas to about
700,000 in Hidalgo.
3.116           Risks. In a project of this complexity risks may arise in the implementation of
different actions and programs. One of the main risks is that the decentralization policy may not be
carried out as forcefully and efficiently as required. To mitigate this risk during project preparation,
detailed and realistic decentralization programs were sought from each of the line secretariats. A
concerted effort between SPP and each of the line secretariats has been agreed upon. However, it is
possible that the states might not accept additional responsibilities. In order to mitigate this risk (a) in
the proposed investment program there are sufficient resources to rehabilitate deteriorated
infrastructure before it is transferred to the states, and (b) institutional development activities would
strengthen the state institutions to receive those tasks. That is the case of rral roads, for which the
states require that the network be transferred in a reasonable state of maintenance, requiring important
investments from the federal government. The Bank would continue to seek through the general
policy dialogue to increase resources for maintenance nationwideP.
3.117          Because the administrative structures of the poorest states are the most deficient, the
institutional ucvraument component is an important instrument for strengthening their capacity.
First, decentralizatiokA -ill be carried out under monitorable conditions and would be accelerated only
in cases that is required for a substantive improvement in services or project operation. Second, the
expansion in the investment programs will be largely dependent on the private sector and contracting.
Third, to strengthen state agc:ies that would either receive additional functions from line agencies or
38.    The Country Economic Menmorndum (CEM) under prpadon makne du. p.. o  a forcefully.



- 52 -
that represent a bottleneck for subproject success, annual institutional development programs would be
developed. Finally, municipalities would be assisted through various programs of administrative
strengthening by the National Institute of Public Administration '(NAP), their capacity for project
formulation by BANOBRAS and state offices to directly provide technical assistance. More details are
provided in Annex 10.
3.118        Another risk could arise from conflicts of interest between the federal and state
governments in the implementation of the state development plans. The CUD mechanism has been
designed for resolving such conflicts, and a high degree of congruency was manifest during project
preparation. Disbursement for the project may not be as fast as desired in view of the enormous
dispersion of subprojects and the need to gather such a large number of CLCs. Project preparation
has streamlined as much as possible the guidelines for aggregation and justification of expenditures in
compliance with Bank rules. Furthermore, decentralization from states to municipalities is being
strongly encouraged by the municipal funds. There is also the risk that some investment decisions
become subject to political influence and money might be misdirected or misappropriated. Most of
the investment program (in terms of specific subprojects) has already been programmed (Annex 4)
using a set of agreed upon guidelines and selection criteria. The audit procedures, carefully designed
for the project, would combine federal, state and independent audits as well as informal methods in
local communities to control the use of funds.
3.119        There is a risk that population pressure and social conflict in the Lacandona Reserve
may impede implementation of the protection plan. Project preparation assisted in the design of
detailed programs and sought actual commitment from the agencies involved. In case such a plan is
not satisfactorily implemented, the Bank would reserve the right to suspend disbursements to Chiapas.

MEXICO
DECENTRALIZATION AND REQIQNAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
FOR THE DISADVANTAGED STATES
Policy Lete
February 27, 1991
Mr. Barber B. Conable
President
World Bank
Dear Mr. President:
Within the context of its development policy, the Govermment of Mexico has attached
special priority to the fight against extreme poverty, an issue that has become more urgent for a large
number of Mexicans as a result of the economic crisis of the last decade.
The National Solidarity Program is the framework for State and community efforts to
confront the symptoms and causes of the poverty affecting
the poorest groups in Mexico.
The Decentralization and Regional Development Project for the States of Chiapas,
Guerrero, Hidalgo and Oaxaca is part of the efforts being made to improve the living conditions of
the population in these jurisdictions, which are among the poorest in Mexico. The project includes
actions in the area of decentralization and institution-building and a substantial increase in the public
expenditure allocated to the development of these States and their Municipalities.
The Federal Government has carried out important functions and resource decentralization
actions in the four project states in sectors such as education, health, agriculture, and agroindustry.
In addition, it has strengthened the decision-making capacity of its offices in the four states.
The Federal Government will continue to support the decentralization policy, mainly in
areas such as transfer of basic health and primary education functions, increasing the states' and
municipalities participation in the operation and maintenance of rural roads, agricultural extension
and transfer of agroindustry infrastructure to producer organizations.
The decentralization of responsibilities towards the project states will be accompanied by
adequate budgetary resources. The Federal Government will continue to support indigenous
populations with resources allocated for these purposes and the budget of the Municipal Solidarity
Funds will be increased to strengthen the management and development .apacity of local governments
and communities.
In order to strengthen the decentralization process to municipalities, the Federal
Government and the participating states channeled Mex$I 10,000 million during 1990 to the 840
municipalities of the four states.



-57 -                               Annex IL
Page 2 of 3
The Government, through the PNS progran, will continue the policy o; decentralization
of the investment program and of transferring support from the federal budget to the states. In 1991
the PNS program will amount to Mex$5. 1 billion, representing 1S percent of the total public
investment program.
To provide impetus to the decentralization strategy and the fight against poverty, the
Mexican Government is taking significant steps in the following areas:
(a) Fiscal Deentralization and AdiusMIeMt ofl thg Tax Receipts System. All the states
included in the project are reviewing their systems for the distribution of tax receipts to
the municipalities, through the system of revenue-sharing. The aim is to make revenue-
sharing more efficient and equitable as well as more transparent. In all states not less
than 20 percent of the General, Complementary and Municipal Funds would be allocated
to municipalities within the agreements of the Fiscal Coordination Law. The state of
liidalg has already reformed its revenue-sharing system. Ihe formula for resource
allocation is contained in the new Fiscal Coordination Law published on December 30,
1989, which came into force on January 1, 1990. ThIs law establishes the new factors
governing the distribution of the Single Receipts Fund to municipalities, as follows: 45
percent of the Fund in direct proportion to the population of each municipality; another
45 percent as a function of the degree of backwardness of each municipality; 5 percent in
line with the amount of Federal tax receipts collected by each municipality; and 5 percent
in line with the number of communities in each municipality. These factors heavily favor
the poor communities. The other three states have initiated the studies required for
modernization of their revenue-sharing formulas in order to achieve the objectives
enunciated above.
(b) Expendiure Planning ana Control. The activities undertaken by the present Federal
Government to strengthen coordination arrangements among Federal, state and municipal
bodies are designed to enhance the machinery and instruments of state, regional and local
planning. Decentralization, simplification and deregulation are fundamental to the
efficient redistribution of functions, responsibilities and resources, and the actions taken
have led to more efficient and flexible management of programs.
(c) Modernization of State Fiscal Systems and Improvement in Tax Collection. In order to
codify and modernize the fiscal systems of the four states, studies have been initiated. In
this area significant progress has already been achieved in the collection of property taxes
ranging from 300 to 800 percent increases during the last fiscal year. In order to
continue improvement of tax collections, all states have already launched programs of
cadastre updating. Ihese programs would start in the most important urban areas where
there is more potential for revenue increase. The states intend also to achieve significant
improvement in collection of water charges.
The Federal Government agrees with and supports the above aims. To further such aims,
the Federal Government commits within the course of the Project to promote, through negotiations
with each of the Project States:
(a) the establishment of an appropriate fiscal framework including codes, collection and
operation of local taxes,

SECRETARtA DE PROGRAMACION
Y PRESUPUESTO
SR. BARBER CONABLE
Banco Mundial
Presidente
Presente.
El Gobierno de la Republica ha asignado dentro de su poiftica de desarrollo, una alta
prioridad al combate a la pobreza extrema, en nuestro pais.
El Programa Nacional de Solidaridad constituye el marco que orienta los esfuerzos quo
realizan el Estado y la Sociedad Mexicana, para enfrentar las manifestaciones y causas
de las condiciones de pobreza de los grupos menos favorecidos.
El Proyecto de Descentralizaci6n y Desarrollo Regional para los Estados de Chiapas,
Guerrero, Hidalgo y Oaxaca, es parte de los esfuerzos que se vienen realizando para
mejorar las condiciones de bienestar de la poblaci6n de esas entidades, las cuales fl9uran
entre las mas pobres de Mexico. El proyecto comprende acciones en matena de
descentralizaci6n y fortalecimiento institucional, asf como un incremento sustanfivo en el
gasto publico asignado al desarrollo de esas entidades y sus municiplos.
El Gobierno Federal ha iniciado un importante proceso de descentralizaci6n de recursos
y funciones hacia esos Estados, principalmente en los sectores de educaci6n, salud,
agricultura y agroindustria. Adicaonalmente, ha reforzado la capacidad de toma de
decisiones de sus delegaciones en estas entidades federativas.
El Gobiemo Federal continuara apoyando la politica de descentralizaci6n, principalmente
en relaci6n a los servicios de salud basica y educaci6n elemental. Propiciari ademas una
mnayor participaci6n do los estados y municipios en la operacion y mantenimiento de los
caminos rura7es y on los sorvicios do extensionismo saola, y facilitar4 la transferencia
do infraestructura agroindustrial a las organizaciones do productores.
La descentralizaci6n de responsabilidades hacia los Estados del proyecto ser6 sustentada
en adecuados recursos presupuestales. El Gobiemo Federal continuarf apoyando el
desarrollo de las comunidades ind(genas asignando recursos para este proposito. Los
Fondos Municipales de Solidaridad seran incrementados para reforzar la capacidad
administrativa y de desarrollo de los gobiemos locales y de sus comunidades. En este
sentido, en 1990, a las 840 municipalikades existentes en los cuatro Estados del proyeco
se canalizaron 110 mil millones de pesos.
