SECIETAARiA OE PROGRAMACION
Y PRESUPUESTO
El Gobierno, a trav6s del Programa Nacional de Solidaridad continuara la poliftica de
descentralizaci6n de los programas de inversi6n y de transferencias para et desarrollo
hacia los Estados. En 1991 el Programa Nacional de Solidaridad cuenta con un
presupuesto autorizado de recursos federales de 5.1 billones de pesos, que representa
el 15% del total del gasto de inversi8n publica federal presupuestal en el pa(s.
Para reforzar la estrategia de descentralizaci6n y el ataque a la pobreza, el Gobierno
Mexicano esti avanzando en las siguientes dreas:
a) DescentralIzacl6n Fiscal y Ajustes en el Slstema de participaclones.
Todos los Estados incluidos en el Proyecto estan revisando sus sistemas de distribuci6n
de participaciones a sus municipios, con el objetivo de hacerlo mas eficiente, equitativo y
transparente.
En todos los Estados no menos del 20% del fondo general y complementario de
articipaciones sera asignado a los municipios, de conformidad con lo establecido en la
Ley de Coordinaci6n Fiscal.
El Estado de Hidalgo ya reform6 su sistema de ingreso-participaci6n y la f6rmula para
asignaci6n de recursos estA contenida en la nueva Ley de Coordinaci6n Fiscal publicada
el 3 de diciembre de 1989, que entr6 en vigor a partir del primero de enero de 1990. Esta
Ley establece los nuevos factores quo regirAn la distribuci6n de las participaciones
generales a los municipios del Estado como sigue: 45% del Fondo en proporci6n directa
a la poblaci6n de cada municiplo; otro 45% en funci6n del grado de marginalidad de cada
municipio; 5% en relaci6n al numero de localidades en cada municipio y 5% en relaci6n
con los impuestos federales captados en cada municipio.
Estos factores favorecen mayormente a las comunidades mAs pobres. Los otros tres
Estados han inicdado los estudios requeridos para modernizar sus f6rmulas de
ingreso-distribuci6n a efecto de alcanzar los objetivosd antes enunciados.
b) PlaneacI6n y Control del gasto.
Las actMidades realizadas por la presente Administraci6n Federal para reforzar la
coordinaci6n entre las estructuras federales, estatales y municipales han sido disefladas
para ampliar la maquinaria y los instrumentos do planeaci6n estatal, regional y local. La



_6!1                     AXtltl'X    I

SECRETARlA DE PROGRAMACION
Y PRESUPUESTO
descentralizaci6n, la simplificaci6n y la desregulaci6n son los elementos basicos para una
eficientre redistribucid6n de funciones, responsabilidades y recursos, y las acciones
realizadas han propiclado un manejo de programas m6s eficiente y flexible.
C) Modernizacl6n de los sistemas flscales estatales y mejoramlento de la
recaudacl6n de Impuestos.
Se han iniciado estudios para codificar y modemizar los sistemas fiscales de los cuatro
Estados. En este sentido se ha logrado un progreso significativo en relaci6n con la
recaudaci6n del impuesto predial con incrementos que van del 300 al 800 por ciento en
el utltimo aiio fiscal. Para continuar mejorando la recaudaci6n fiscal, todos los estados
han iniciado programas de actualizaci6n catastral. Estos programas se iniciaran en las
areas urbanas mas importantes donde existe mayor potencial para incrementar la
recaudaci6n.
Los Estados tambien esperan alcanzar un mejoramiento sustantivo en maeeria de
recaudaci6n por cuotas de consumo de agua.
El Gobierno Federal esta de acuerdo y apoya los objetivos anteriores. Para fomentar tales
objetivos, el Gobierno Federal se compromete durante el desarrollo del proyecto a realizar
negociaciones con cada uno de los Estados para que:
i) Se establezca una estructura fiscal apropiada que comprenda c6digos y reglamentos
para la recaudacion y operaci6n de los impuestos locales.
ii) A trav6s de la SPP y con la participaci6n de la SHCP, se realicen ajustes en los
esquemas de asignaci6n de recursos en los cuatro Estados del Proyecto, revisando
Ia f6rmula a efecto de cumplir con los lineamientos de la Ley de Coordinaci6n Fiscal
y hacerla mis transparente y equitativa.
Metas del proceso de Descentrallzacl6n y Desarrollo.
Para asegurar que se alcancen los objetivos del proyecto en los cuatro Estados, el
Gobierno Mexicano prev6 realizar lo siguiente:

lo asignado a cada uno de ellos en 1989, y Para finales del ahlo fiscal de 1993, la suma
de la contribuci6n federal total der;vada del CUD en los cuatro Estados participantes

2. Incrementar los Fondos Municipales en los cuatro Estados del proyecto, en no menos

3. Continuar la pol(tica de descentralizaci6n del programa de inversiones y la transferencia

4. Permanentemente revisar la normatividad establecida para el gasto transferido a efecto

3. Sg Munic9 Fund
14.               'Me Municipal Fund comprised the remaining 2 percent of revenue-hing funds. It is

not clear that this fund should be considered as other than as another compto t of general federa-state revenue
saring, esstially supemtary to the General Fund.
1S.               1be pre-19       system had a number of shortcomings. Clearty, it had little redistributive
component built into it. It divetged from an equal per capita allocation in favor of an allocation which is close
to one basd aon per cpita consumption lvls with one major exception. That mnjor exception was that the
1980 petroleum revenues accruing to the states receiving them is permanently built in. Thus, the system did
little to address the inefficiencies and inequities resulting ftom a federal system in which different states have
different financial capabilities.
The 1990 Axtaeements
16.               The amendments to the revenue sharing arrangements introduced in November 1989 represent
a significant change in the allocation formula, and one which clearly improves the system."
17.               The proportionat amount of federal tax revenues to be shared would remain the same in
aggregate-18.1 percent. The previous General fund and Compensatory Fund are amalgamated into one fund
with a completely new allocation fonmula. Fifty percent of the fund is to be allocated on a strict per capita
.        The nraw Ly de Coordinacidn Fisal defines the new revenue-sharing criteria, which, for the General Fund, has two major
componnts   follows  (a) 50 percet shall, by 1994, be distributed in propottion to the number of people in a given state; and
(b) the other 50 percent shll be distributed using a coefficient based on the conribuciones asinables (c.a.) for each state.
(hese include federal tax on new cats; state charges and taxes on the use of vehicles, prm'iction and service charges on
petroleum products, bottled water, alcoholic beverages, beer, and tobacco products; and n micipal property taxes and water
charges.) The coefficient is calculated in this mauer: firtt, the amount received in the previous year by the state from this
portion of e Genetal Fund is multiplied by a fraction, the curreat c.a. divided by the cs. from two years previous. Obviously
this fraction would be greater than one if tax collections have increased, lesa than one if it declines. Call the products of these
ftations and the previous yeats General Fund allocations for each state X]. Then, the figures XY obtained in the first step are
summed and the perceatsge contibuted to that sum is calculated for each state. Tis percentage is the proportion of the second
50 percent of the Geteral Fund the tate will receive. 'n. amount available in the General Fund is 18.1 nercent of all federal
restoures from all taxes and chaes. Twenty percent of the allocations are earmaked for the municipalities, but the states
choose how and where to distribute this pottion.
The Municipal Fund is now distributed to the sta, also earmarked for municipal disbursement at each state's discretion, in a
mfn    smiba to the way Compenaory Fund previously waSt   receive funds from the Municipa Fund in the invers
propoton of the mwnt given to the state per capita out of the General Fund. The Municipal Fund repesents 0.42 pe.ceat of
all f6deral Inconsw with the exception of that derved from ome 'derecho extrordinario' levied on minerals and hydrocarbons.
One might nome the the net effect of this method is the earmarking of federal asisance to municipalities in some inverse relation
to both tat and municipal revenue collections ad state populatio. This is considered reditibutive.
The law also provides for the tfr-.ition period 1991-1994 as follows, where Pat I and Pan 2 refer to the portion of the General

basis gradualy after a four-year transition period in which the proportio allocated per capita rises by 10
percent per year starting from an initial 10 percent. Correspondingly, that allocated by historic shares, as
discussed next, falls from 90 percen to 50 percent gradually.
18.           'Me remaining 50 percent is to be allocated by a method which again resorts to the historic
shares of the existing General Fund. In the initial year, the allocation is detrmined precisely by the allocation
of the General Fund. Each subsequent year the share of each state rises according to the relative rate of
increase of a variety of taxes collected within the stte. The taxes used include the excise taxes on peeroleum,
water vehicles, alcohol and tobacco, as well as water levies and property taxes. The excise taxes are all
federally admnistered. The later two sources of revenue are iacluded to provide some incentive for states (and
their municipalities) to improve revenue-raising efforts in these areas. Each year the share of a state rises or
falls in proportion to the extent to which state revenues from these sources rise or fall relative to the national
average.
19.           This method of adjusting the shares corresponds with the method that was used under the
General Fund except that, instead of basing the adjustment on VAT revenues co11ected in the state (by the state),
it is based on excise and property taxes within the state. Thus, the deleterious administrative competition for
VAT revenues was eliminated. Stats did, however, retain an audit function under the VAT alongside the
federal government. As an inducement to fulfill this requirement, they are to receive a share of the audit
procees.
20.            The Municipal Fund will remain, but its allocation formula is to change. Intead o, using the
General Fund allocation as before, the allocation is now to be based on the inverse of hie allocation described
above CIris is the formula previously used under the Compensatory Fund). As before, the Municipal Fund is
of relatively small size and importance. Though it incorporates some redistributive component, it is relatively
ineffective because it is not targeted to the most needy states.
21.            The sharing of import taxes and petroleum export duties continues to be shared with the states
containing petroleum refining and exporting cities.
II. Between States and Municigalities
22.            The rules to define revenue-sharing from states to municipalities are set by each state
legislature. Under the Ley de Coordinaci6n Fiscal states must transfer to municipalities at least 20 percent of
their revenue sharing in the General and Complementary Funds and the total of the Municipal Fund. Allocation
rules have been set each year and sometimes change significantly, making revenue projections by municipalities
quite difficult.
23.            Most of the states seek to distribute the part of the General and Complementary Funds to the
municipalities based on tax collections of VAT and some use the Municipal Fund for redistributive purposes.
The state of Mexico uses the taxes collected by each municipality as a basis to allocate the General Fund. Both
the state of Mexico and Nuevo Leon use population as a basis for allocating both the General and
Complementary Funds.
24.            This is an area which merits further improvements aiming at: (a) making revenue sharing to
municipalities more tnsparent and predictable; (b) using a combination of rules to allocate funds based on the
fiscal basis and an equity criteria to bring poor municipalities up to a minimum standard for provision of local
public goods.

STE ADMUNICIPAL FINANCES
SItme Finnce
1.            The four sources of finacing of stdae expitrwes ae:
O          Revenue sharing through fedeally coUlected taxes.
O          Locl rsources obtined throu  local taation.
O          Recumrrent expendiures (presupsto noml) tansfefred from the federal budget.
o          Transfers through the Conveoio U      for rual invesments, social sectors, and other
infratre; negotiated every year with SPP.
2.             Curent state budget expenditures are covered mainly by participation in VAT collection, of
which 30 percent is transfeed to the ae. The system has some similarities with the West German fedeml
sysem, but in the latter case almost 75 percent of VAT is distributed to states, which also share a large amount
of personal and company income taxes. In Mexico, the major proportion of public expenditures (current and
capitl) in the states stil belongs to the normal program of federl agencies.
3.             The grant system for investment (conditional and specific grants) through the CUD is similar
to the US system in certain aspects, but in the US investment planning and budgeting are decentalized
completely. In most of the federated indusalimzed countries, the states share revenues with the central
govemment, but there are no distinctions between transfers for sate investment and current expenditures.
4.             The structure of state revenue financing has changed sbstatally in the last six years. After
the 1979 fiscal reform the share of state taxes decreased from 25.3 percent to 9.1 percent, while revenue-
shig increased from 24.8 percent to 43.3 percent its contribution to state financing, as state taxes were
replaced by fedenl taxes. The ttansfer of property tax from the staste to municipal govemments further eroded
the tax capacity of states, as its hae decresed 9.1 in 1981 to 2.0 pert in 1984. The role of business
licenses and other smilar taxes has also decreased by about 4 pcentage points in the same period, while fees
for public services incresed by about the same percentage. Revenues frm state enterprises and other property
has also been reduced by about four perctage points (from 1980 to 1985).' The share of borrowing has
remained at about 8 percent of the total. Federd subsidis to cover state budget deficits run at about 2.5 percent
of the total financing, but they are concentmted in 7 of the 31 states.
S.             As it would be expected, richer states have a higher tax capability. In the richer four states,
local taxes represent about 2.1 percent of the total financing in 1985, cost wecovery from public services 3.7
pet and property income about 10 percent. For the four poorest stes aUl those sources are negligible,
except pwperty income in Guemro, due to Acapldco (7.5 pet of the total). Quite puadoxically, federal
subsidies have benefitted more the ncher than the poorer states, while stat borowmg has been more important
for tho poorer.
t.     An dda in Xhs setion is bond on nINw, Fbms Pblia  E_a  y Mualips de Mxd*co, 1976-1985, Meico City, 1988.

6.             On-a pet capt basis, staic incomes fiom their ows  sourcs, i.e.,- local taxes and fees from
provision of local servics, vary slufii.ntly among states (data for 1985). Four types of states have a larger
tax capability. The richust states am the Northern industrial states where the muquiladora has had a major
impact like Baja California and Nuevo Laon. The states with oil exploitation like Tabasco, Tamaulipas,
Camipeche and Veracruz. The states with large conminercial and administrative urban centers like the DF and
alisco. And, finally, the states with large tourism complexes like Colima and Quintana Roo. The poorest are
Hidalgo, Michoacan, Oaxaca and Nayarit. A very low correlation exists between per capita income and actual
tax revenues per capita, which confirms studies done for other couatries and reflects differences in tax basis and
differences in the preferences for public goods.
7.             There was a deterioration in the redistributive capability of the state budgets. In fact, total
incomes of the upper half states (richer) increased by 24.6 percent, on a weignted average, while the lower half
increased only by 10 percent.
8.             Empirical research on the US revenue sharing system has demonstrated that about two thirds
of the revenue sharing appropriations is used for various substitution purposes: more than one half was used for
cutting local taxes and borrowing and the rest used to maintain existing programs and prevent budget cuts.3 An
econometric study has also shown that increases in revenue-sharing were associated with a deceleration in
collection of taxes locally controlled like property taxes and service fees, on the revenue side and increase in
capital expenditures on the expenditure side.'
9.             Similar results are observed for Mexico. First, the reduction of 1980 in own taxes was part of
the agreement between the federal and state governments. However, the erosion of the state own resources
continued. In our view, is the result of two factors, besides the ones noted elsewhere. First, that there was not
enough decentralization of services and responsibilities from the federal to state governments that could take the
additional res-;rces. Second, that state govermments preferred to decrease local taxes instead of augmenting
local expenditures. In the 1980 to 1985 period, only four states (Campeche, Colima, Aguascalientes and Nuevo
Leon, all rich states) increased their own resources, in real per capita terms.
Municipal Finances
10.            The structure of municipal financing changed also significantly in the first half of the 1980s.
Revenue-sharing from the federal government increased from 26.3 percent in 1980 to 45.8 percent in 1982 and
further to about 60 percent of the total municipal incomes after 1983. That increase was mirrored in a decrease
in all categories of own resources. In 1985, local taxes financed about 12 percent of the budget, licenses about 6
percent, sevice fees 5 percent, property income 11 percent and borrowing covered 4.5 percent.
I!.            The weighted average of total revenues of municipalities, on a per capita basis, and at 1980
prices, increased from Mex$395 in 1980 to about Mex$492 in 1985. The redistributive impact did not change
during the period, since both the upper and lower half of the states had a similar increase.
12.            There was an almost perfect correlation between the increase in revenue-sharing and
expenditures by the municipalities in the 1980-85 period. In that period, the municipalities with the largest
growth were in the states of Zacatecas, Colima, Oaxaca, Campeche, Puebla and Tlaxcala. However, there was
again, significant substitution between revenue-sharing and own resources. Own resources decreased from
Mex$282 per capita in 1980 to about Mex$189 in 1985. Except for four states, municipalities in all other states
decreased their own resources. The pattem of own resources shows an increase with income per capita, with
2.     ft.P. Nathan, A. Mwe! end S. Culkins, Monitoinng Revenue Sharng, Brooki   tution, Washington DC, 1975, pp
232-233.
3.     D. Caputo and R. Cole ed. Revenue Sharing, Lexington Books, 1976, pp. 61.

Page 3 of 4
some outliers referring mainly to oil states or states with tourism comnpexes. The largest drops and increases in
own resources were observed for richer states, reflecting again mainly difterences in preferences for public
goods.
Property Taxes
13.            The yields of the property tax have declined dranatically during this decade. In real terms,
property tax revenues diropped by 60 percent between 1980 and 1984. More recent figuires from individual
municipios suggest this trend has continued. This decline is attributable to two factors. First, the high inflation
of the 1980s has not been reflected in property tax assessments. Since 1980, the rate of inflation in Mexico has
totalled nearly 1005 percent. Property assessments, based originally on prices of the 1970s--have been adjusted
erratically, and at level far below the rate of inflation. Second, states have been lax in updating changes in the
physical characteristics of they tax base--new construction, subdivisions, and changes in land use-particularly
since the property tax was decentralized to the municipal level. As a result, major gaps in the coverage of the
tax base now exist.
14.            From a technical standpoint, Mexico is relatively well-position to revive its property tax.
Compared to other countries at similar levels of development (Brazil), the adnministrative apparatus for property
taxation is in good condition. States invested heavily in urban tax mapping and the mass appraisal during the
1970s. These maps and property records, while outdated, would provide a sound basis for a general resurvey
and revaluation of properties. Financing for such revaluations is currently available from BANOBRAS and has
been used, with apparent success, in Merida (Yucatan) and San Luis Potosi.
i5.            The present institutional arrangements discourage such efforts, however. Any mayor wishing
to undertake a general resurvey, adjust assessments, or increase tax rates, must obtain the approval of both the
state governor and the stage congress. All three political actors must agree: any one of them can exercise a
veto. Such a convergence of views likely to be rare. From the state's perspective, the political costs of
authorizing an increase in property taxes are high, and the political benefits are few, the tax provides the state
with no additional revenue.
16.            One means of reducing the political constraints on the property tax would be to permit
assessment to be indexed to inflation. Automatic inflation-indexing would permit assessments to remain
constant in real terms, witheut requiring any deliberate action on the part of the govemor, mayor, or congress.
The State of Yucatan has already taken this step: beginning January 1990, the pricing factors used in
calculating assessments will be automatically increased at half the rate of increase in the minimum wage.
17.            There may also be merits in decentralizing the administration of the property tax. Municipal
governments would presumably take a more active interest in administering their principal source of
discretionary income. Opinions differ on this issue, however. Earlier Bank studies have suggested that smaller
munici2ios would not be technically capable of administering the tax. Some municipal officials argue that a
decentralized tax would be too vulnerable to local politics. Experience in other developing countries shows that
both centralized and decentralized administrations have succeeded in some instances and failed in others. The
choice of administrative arrangements does not appear to guarantee either outcome. In general, however, the
incompetence of local govenmment appears to be more easily mitigated thaa the indifference of central
authorities.
18.            Municipal governments should also be given greater latitude in setting tax rates. If the
property tax is to serve as the means by which local taxpayers express their effective demand for municipal
services, local governments must have the power to adjust local tax levels accordingly.



-82 -                                    Annx 3
Page 4 of 4
ot Recovea of L4al Pic Sevic
19.           Fees and charges contribute about ten percet to total municipal revenue. Roughly half of this
is produced by fees, the remaider by charges. Fees-imposed for plawing and building permits, regisratn of
births and maniage, etc.--should not be viewed as a potential source of municipal revenue. Any atlempt to ue
these adnunstratve charges as sources of genera revenue would discourage compline with reguao
procedures.
20.            There are strong arguments for increasing the yields of user chawges, however. At prsent,
the level of these charges-for water supply, markets, saht s, ad other municipal facilities-are wel
below the costs of service delivery. In 1987, water charges on industri and comerci consumes wee
estimated to cover only 30 percent of the costs of sevice delivery. Subsidies take the fonn of transfes from
state or municipal general revenues (to cover operatng losses) and below-markt or gant financing of capital
investments. This implies both an inefficient level of services provision and an inequitable pattern of subsidies:
from general taxpayers to those with access to subsidized services.
21.            The constraints on increasing the yields of charges are similar to thse confionting local taxes.
Approval must be given by swveral different political entities, any of which can exercise a veto. Inflation has
made the task more difficult, by requiring a major annual increase if tariffs are to keep up with increases in the
costs of service delivery. The solutions may also be similar increasing loca latitude in tariff-setting and
indexing user charge levels to infation.
22.           In the case of water supply, increasing local atitude over tariffs could also reduce a second
source of inefficiency in water pricing. At present, s_ta-detemined water tariffs do not refect vaiations in the
costs of delivenng water in different mnicigi: tates are uniform for eatire states or for groups of m  unicip
within a state. As a result, water consumption is subsidized in those locations where it is most expensive to
deliver.

18.   The municipal fund group could only finance small projects of local impact (Type A projects). The

19.   The regional fund group would finance medium-sized projects (Type B). It would be allocated among

20.   The state investment group would finance Type C projects (lage with a state-wide impact). It would be
distributed according to the following criteria:
(a) economic rate of return of the project; and
(b) income distribution impact.
That is, if there are two projects with the same rate of return, the one in a poorer area would have priority over
the one in a richer area. The one in the richer area could be undertaken if the financing ftom the beneficiaries
compensates for the redistribution effect.
21. For equalization purposes the municipal fund group would be distributed among municipalities on a
matching basis, with a larger municipal contribution from the richer municipality: e.g., a poor one would
contribute with 25 percent and a rich one with 75 percent.
22. While revenue sharing is a block grant system, the cu.-rent system of investment grants is mainly
composed of matching and selective grants. Since the system of revenue sharing is supposed to be improved
simultaneously, municipalities would be able to cover most of the curseat costs that investment projects will
generate. Besides, they would also have the capability to match part of the project costs. Such a rule is also
important to ensure better project efficiency and tax effort.
23.  In the short term, the formula would provide the budget consttaint for municipalities so the matching
shoul( be only on an overall basis with the proportion of the municipality contributing proportional to a poverty
indicator. Over time, the needs may arise to introduce in the municipal funds some selective matching grants
cnteria to guide the muicipal preferences towards state objectives. However, in the short term, the system
mrst be simple.
C. Proosd System for Processing Small Proiects'
Introduction
24.    The present SPP syhem of processing small projects focuses primarily on technical feasibility and
provides little guidetines on how to pnoritize these project requests. This results in great variation in project
screening, proect design quality and in almost all projects aving equal priority. Under such circumste,
planners often resort to cutting resoures in not the most optimal manner. TIese weaknesses will be greatly
reduced once appraisal procedures are standardid to include cetain policy and efficiency consideradons and,
for productive projects, the use of abbreviated benefit/cost analysis are required.
25.    This paper outlines steps for improvng the processiag of small project rqus   submitted to PNS for
fincing. It defines the objective, required modifications in the project cycle, the project selection process,
use of proposed seection criteia, and how the evised system could assist p    invesments. It

Objective
26.     The purpose of the project selection system is to permit states to deine a prgram of priorities
through a simple but more systematic and objective technique of selecting projects before they reach the
COPLADEs and SPPlMexico.' Exclusion and prioritization criteria designed specifically for PNS programs,
and if necesary sub-programs, would be used to screen and ultimately select projects. The states would
construct their respective programs based on project ranings and submit to SPP/Mexico these programs as
opposed to the detailed project lists.
27.     Advantages of the system would include: (a) a quicker, easier way of identifying projecs which qualify
for PNS financing (b) a basis for ptiority ranking and programming such investments; (c) application of
uniform screening by municipalities; and (d) improved quality of project design. Communities would know the
criteria by which their requests would be lassesed, the reasons for de-seection, and the options that are open
for submitting reformulated or new proposals. Eventually, a certain knowledge base and expertise would be
developed at the local level of many municipalities. This would reduce the workload at state level and permit
more attention to be devoted to in-depth analysis of medium and larger projects (i.e., those above USS18,000
equivalent). PNS is curtly examining how to screen projects electronically using an appropriate expert
systems (artificial intelligence) package.'
Proiect Cyvgle
28.     Project identification and preparation would continue to be the responsibility of communities and
municipalities, as under the present system. The proposed changes focus on  ect aggtial when
municipalities, state planning agencies and SPPADelegations would be the main actors. Their functions and the
proposed steps for appraising project requests am summrized in Attachment 1. If municipalities lack the
capacity to screen projects, assstance could be provided by state planning agencies, SPP/regions or other
technical assistance arrangements. Other phases of the project cycle, (i.e., preparation of the exgedientes
telnicos, project approval, execution, supervision, monitoring and evaluation) emnain unchanged and follow the
steps outlined in the PNS Operations Manual.

35.    Ihe quality of the sdection process will depend on: ka) the clarity vith which sectoral stategies and
guidelines for the selection criteri are defined; (b) knowledge and uw  policy makers have of these;
and (c) the extent to which such steges wre communicated to municipalities and incorporated by them into
their own decision-maing.
36.    The pruject selection system will be tried for six months in the four states with a limited number of
pogram categories for the Municipal Solidarity Funds (see Annex 8). Adjustments in the basic project form
and criteria would need to be made in light of this experience. After six to eight months, an appraisal should be
ccnducted of the effectivees of the system and how it might be further steadmlined, e.g. gradual introduction
of electronic processig and development of a tailored expert system  lhe system's tngger points (scores
assigned to each component [sectorad piority, efficiency, Urgeting, etc.]; minimal score rle; and the guidelines
regarding program/subrograim and type of projects [productive, social infrsuctul distribution) would
especialy need careful attention. The system should hen be used for prepaing the FY91 and PY92 investment
programs.

DECENTRALIZATION A4ND REGIONAL DEVELFOPMENTPR
FOR THE DISSDNA S6IE{
RULES EQR MMMR2JQ= ANwOMENTALS ASSESSMENT
1.            The legal framework for environmn  assesmeat (EA) of subprojects is provided by the Ley
GeCeal de Equilibrio Ecologico y Proteccion aI Ambiente (1988). This federal law lagely reflects the Bank
opetion  dectives on envirnmnt assesmet. A study was caued out durig prMect preparation and its
recommdations were approved by SEDUE and SPP (CQ,ngente Ambientbl: Provecto de Dewentralizacion v
Desoollo Regional) which lays down the nres for screening and conducting eavironmental assessments under
the Decentralization and Regional Development Project. Moreover, it defies an institutional strngthening
program to assure satisfactory quality for the analysis and poLicies proposed in the four states.
2.             The initial screening of projects by type of EA required is canried out by the Ecology
Subcommittee of COPLADES. For subprojects requiring an intermediate level of EA, the subcommittee would
inform the respective executing agency. For subprojects requiring a specific and more developed EA, the
subcommittee would subcontract to local consulting firms, universities or other recognized institutions
previously approved by SEDUE. SEDUE, through state delegations, is the agency that would give final
aprval of the EA to be carried out.
3.             According to article 5 of the Reglamento of the above law, the following subprojects are
exempt ftom EA: (a) construction and demolition of buildings in urban zones; (b) conservation and
rebabilitation of buildings; (c) dams of less than 500,000 cubic meters; (d) irrigation systems of less than 100
ha; (e) wells and systems to store water with less than 10 percent of annual precipitation; and (f) conservation
and rehabilitation in general.
4.             The following re minimum environmental rqirements to be applied under EA:
(a)    Preventing and Controlling Water Pollution and Rational Use of Water: construction of
systems for treatment of residues in water, recycling of treated water; storage of water from
rainfall in small dams or reservoirs; control of use of agro-chemicals; control of expansion of
urban areas to fertile soils, forests and natural reserves; soil conservation; appropriate
sewerage and treatment of wastes; reforestion; preservation of natur reserves and
endangered species.
(b)    Rural and Feeder Roads: adequate the use of materials with the degree of run-off
precipitation; works for soil conservation in the area bordering the road; avoid changing
naturl water flows; avoid deforestation; avoid intensive exploitation of materials.
(c)    Water Sunoly Systes: planning and implementation of the coexisting sewerage systems; re-
ue of treated waters; monitoring of water quality.
(d)    Rural Electrification: avoidance of deforestation or other distrance to ecological systems.
S.             Further aspects are discussed in Annex 8 on the Environment Component and in the worldng
Papr

COST RECOVERY RULES FOR SROJECTS FINANCED BY THE PROOED PROJEC
1.    The Bank will only disburse, under this proposed project, for subprojects which follow the cost-recovery policies
(of both investment costs and operations and maintenmace costs) already agreed with the goverment in other opeiations.
These cost-recovery policies are those agreed with in the water supply sector (proposed Water Supply and Sanitation
Sector Project), irrigation subsector (proposed Irrigation and Dainage Sector Loan) and the government's poverty
strategy (for which PRONASOL is the implementing arm) and are as follows:
(a) Water supply projects wiU follow cost recovery mles detailed in the proposed Water Supply and Sanitation
Project.' The GOM bas agreed to link PRONASOL lending in the water and sanitation sector to the same criteria
and norms designed for that loan; a similar condition has been included in this loan for the provision of project
funding. For investment costs, these rules are based on CONAPO's povety index (PI) which is used to
determine both the maximum federal and state assistance and the minimum participation of credit component and
internal cash generation in the financial mix of funds of each subproject at the municipal level. Total intel
cash generation will, in average, range from 15 percent in 1990 to 25 percent of cost in 1994. The PI clifies
municipalities into three poverty categories: High, Medium and Low, and the ceilings for federal and tat
participation and floors for internal cash generation (with credit being the residual) are as follows:
?L               Fdergl and Sat            Bmneficianis               GOdiA
High                   80                     20                       0
Medium                 50                      15                       3S
Low                    35                      15                       50
Exceptions to the above rules are made in the Water Supply and Sanitation Sector loan, but they refer to sectoral
policies and therefore do not apply to thi- proposed project. The annual Sector Review in November of each year
redefines limits to be applied the next year. For O&M costs, the rule is 100 percent recovery (except depreciation)
from the first year.'
(b) Irrigatjon projects will follow cost recovery concepts as contained in the CUDIPNS schedule of the next table.
One hundred percent of O&M cost recovery would be required for all projects financed by the Bank.
(c) Other Proiects (mainly agricultural, agroindustrial, marketing and housing subprojects) will follow cost recovery
guidelines indicated in the SPP Manual Unico de Qoeracion. Promrama de Solidaridad. It is estimated that
beneficiaries' participation in investment costs (cash, labor and materials and credit) will range between 25 and
70 percent, depending on the sector and location of the subproject. Table 1 expands on the Manual's annex A
'Programas con recuperacion' for these subprojects, and indicates the percentages of coverage of investment costs
expected from the federal, state and participants' contribution in the form of credit. Participants' expected
contribution, besides credit (or cash equivalent) is requested by the state to the beneficiaries groups. It can be in
cash, local materials and community (free) labor. If it is in the form of cash or local materials it can be
discounted towards the state contribution.4
1. Sec Mexico. SAR for Water Suloly and Sanitation Proict. Repon No. 7726-ME, Annex 2.
2. For low income communities (3igh PI), community participation for constrotion is accounted as internal cash generation.
3. For community selcction crieria, see SAR, Womcn. Water and Dveclooment, pages 7-8 and Annex I.

DECENTRALIZATION AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENI PROJECI
FOR THE DISADVR       GED STATES
OPERATION AND MAINTENACE GUIDELINES
1. Maintenance of Municrial Roads
Legal Asts
1.    Responsibility for construction and maintenance of terdary municipsl roads and feeder roads should be
shifted from federal to municipal authorities. Therefore a legal definition of municipal roads consistent with a
functional -jurisdictional classification of roads is required. Presendy, no such definition exists. An inventory
then should be undertaken to determine the functional-jurisdictional road classification for all networs other
than federal highways or state highways. Finally an inventory of the characteristics of roads in physical tenr
should be carried out at municipality level. This last exercise would provide critical information for drawing up
a program for developing local capacity and for shifting fill maintenance and mangemet responsibilities to
municipalities within a realistic time frame. However tranusitional prognmmatic measures would be taken under
the Pwogram.
2.   Under the program assurance would be provided at negotiations that prior to the initiation of any
municipal road project, SPP would enter into agreements with the municipality, defining the provisory transr
of such roads to the municipal road network and defining road maintenance responsibilities and sources and
level of funding for the maintenance of municipal roads undergoing improvement or construction.
Routine Maintenance
3.    Appropriate technology is as important to maintenance as it is for constuction. The feeder road have
been built with low technology as to cause minimum obstruction to the elements, primarily rain water. The
alignments adapt to the terain and do not have expensive protective featrs of bigh cass roads to withstand the
elements. Routine maintenance consists of simple work items regularly performed througbout the year. Theses
activities consist in maintaining the drainage system (ditches, culvert, ford, v igetation) and the running surface
(flling potholes and ruts, maintaining the surface camber).
4.   The program would support a labor based strategy to handle these routine maintenance activities.
Among the different possible system, maintenance by force account with permanently employed laborrs, would
be discouraged. Three other systems would be encouraged: (a) the communlOy contst  signed ben
the municipal'ty and a legally constituted association; (b) the   that involves a contract between
a local individual and the municipality, whereby the individual receives paynent to carry all routine maintenance
over a fixed length of road using municipal-fumnised hand tools and were payment in contingent to satisfctlory
performance; and (c) self-hbelp maItenance system with expenses limited to supply of hand tools whvre the
residents bave a strong sense and history of community betterment activities and were the population perceives
the benefits from maintenance as accruing specifically to themselves.
Fundin? and Organizational Aspeas
S.    The Municipal Fund would be the main channel to finance routine maintenance under type A or B
projects (small- or medium-sized projects). A set of typical contract agreements between municipalities and
constituted associations or lengthmen woldd be signed with the _mitance of the Federa/State highway ageci
resposible for the tutelge of the municipalities. These contracts would follow the ageed nrle set  for type
A or B projects.



-99-
Page 2 of 3
6.   It is estinated that the expeaditur  for routine mAintenaee of municipa nu  rwads under the prgam
would be annually of about US$300 per km, equivalent to about US$10 million over a four-year period.
7.    The labor-based maintenance strategy based on contract through the Munmipal Fund and the principle of
financing routine maintenance of muoicipal roads improved under the progrm would be agreed at negotiations.
Periodic Maintenance
8.    Periodic matntenance consists of more extensive maintenuace operions that are required only every
several years such as reshaping running surface or rgrveluig.  he objective of these maitenance activities is
to restore a facility more or less to its original condition but appropriate to the currt level of traffic which
may be much larger than its original desig level; these activities can therefore be coosdered as capital
improvement. Spot improvement programs may well fall under this category of periodic mtenamnclapital
improvement. Therefore, thie Program in fact alteady takes into consideration some form of periodic
maintenance for a scheduled equivalat of US$15 million.
9.    A system would be progressively established to institutionalize and develop periodic maintenance. A
strategy would be designed considering the various alternatves (a) to assess maintenance requirements in terms
of necessary capabilities and funds and to modify or not existing instituions to meet the needs; and (b) to
implement periodic maintenance by cop-tract through simplified contracting procedures whenever the possibility
of developing small contractors exists.
10.   The progrm, through its various proposed mechanisms, would help the definition of the above strategy,
and in particular through the decentralization program the possibility to set up a specific unit within the road
maintenance services of the state/federal highway agencies to assist municipalities. T!e implementation of the
spot improvement program would also constitute by itself quite a training exercise from which lessons to
promote use of appropriate periodic maintenance technology would be derived.
11.  It would be agreed during negotations hat SPP would prst to the Bank the draft of a comprehensive
maintenance strategy for municipal roads by January 15, 1991, a detailed plan of action to develop specific
actions on periodic maintenance by June 30, 1992, and starting the implemetaton of the plan by Sepmber 30,
1992.
II. Institutional Arranuements for O&M of Rual Waw SUD1V projects
12.         The proposed loan would have the same oprations and maintenance (O&M) cost-recovery
requimments as the Water and Sanitation Sector Loan, namely 100 percent cost recovery from the fitst year.
For this reason, it is importnt to put in place efficient and adequate insttutional argements to carry out the
O&M to ensure sustainability of these services once the systems are in place. This Annex details the institutional
arrangements (criteria and process for community slection as well as community's responsibilities) to attain the
sustainability goal.
13.         The capabilities of the vilages to opeate and main ther watr and santatio systems is not
totally adequatL o. & re sustainability of the work. For this reason, the communities sould be carefully
selected, the State WaL- Agency (SWA) should provide training to alow the community to carry out its
responsibilites, and the community should assume its responsibilities and pay the water tariffs needed to
maintain and operate the systcm. Since the community cannot acquire all the specialid tainng necessty to
carry out the more technical aspects of O&M, it would contract either with the State Water Companies or with
private firms to carry out this morm intricate O&M works, and the community would defray the cost of these
contracts with the proceeds of water tariffs.



100-                                    Anneg 6
Page 3 of 3
14.         The community selection follows the metbodology included in the ptoposed WomeQ, Water and
Development Project (WWDY. This methodology reflects both: (a) the recognition ofthe importace of
community panicipation, encouraging self-help and promoting incred community responsibilities; and (b) the
Government's strategy to change the generalized perception that improvemeots in living standards can only be
achieved through public spending. Therefore the selection would consist of two steps:
(a)   Screening of potential communities, based on secondary data, according to the following criteria:
(i) estimated cost of bringing the wa oased on chaacteristics of the water source; (ii) degree
of community marginality; and (iii) Ia <.n participatory experiences in the conununity; and
(b)   applying selection criteria based on: (i) the estimated cost of the respective water supply and
sanitation subprojects (a maximum per capita investment cost of US$150 for both water and
sanitation systems); (ii) community's oommitment to the prject's activities.
15.         In the same spirit of PRONASOL of mustering community organization to constitute a
Development Committee, the water supply subprojects will constitute water committees as indicated in the
paragraph below. SPP/COPLADES staff should, like the SWA staff in the WWD project, be trained in
community mobilization techniques and facilitate the community to participate and assume the responsibilities
that go along with the execution of the investment.
16.         The community, with the help of the SPP/COPLADES agent, would be responsible for the
following: (a) organizing the water committees and selecting the local water careaer and (b) paying lhe water
tariffs. The local water committee would be responsible for, in agreement with the community: (a) assessing
the availability of its own financial and non-financial resources; (b) defining local preferences, abilities and
willingness to commit its resources; (c) agreeing with the State Water Agency on the level of service to be used
for their water and sanitation systems; and (d) contracting with the SWA or the private sector for O&M work
that they cannot carry out themselves. The water caretaker would: (a) carry out minor maintenance works; (b)
inform the State Water Agency on major problems requiring their involvement; (c) collect water tariffs weekly
(to take into account their low-saving capability of the beneficiaries) and deposit the payments in an account
earmarked for water system needs; and (d) promote community meetings to evaluate the operation of the system
and suggest future extension and/or upgrading.
1.   See SAR, Water. Women and Develooment Project, pages 7-5 and Alnnex 1.

ZgENTRALIZATION AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT

REiCKUKE   CQUS IMPLICATIONS OF INVESTMENT PROGRAMS
1.            This Annex estimates the recurrent costs with budgetrry implications of the proposed
investment programs in the four states for the 1990-94 period. The investment progrms with larger irLact on
the  current budge. mvould be the roads component for the maintenance required for newly conructed roads
and the education component for the personnel required to staff the indiaucture to be bWult under the prqject.
For agriculture projects, the maintence is to be financed by the beneficiaries and the impact on recurrent costs
in terms of extension and technical assisne can be easily absorbed by the delegations of SARH. For water
supply projects, maintenance is also to be financed by beneficiaries. The cost for operaton and mintenance in
small water projects is estimated at about US$5 million per annum, representing about five percet of
investment costs and equivalent to about US$25-35 per beneficiay and per annum.
2.            In the case of roads the total estimated impact would be in the four states US$7 million per
annum, after the five-year program. Such estimates are based on technical parameters, asmLig a continuous
maintenance plus a five-year period major maintenance work, taking as a base the total cost of now and
significantly rehabilitaed roads. Under the program of decentraizion to be undertaken i the course of the
project, the financing required for the road maintenance would be budgeted in SCT Progam  Normal,
repesenting about 10 percnt of that budget, and transferred as a block grant to the state that than would
transfer to the regional and municipal executing agencies the required amounts.
3.            The increase in oducational infrasture requires the creation of new posts for teachers as
well as some iieae in administratve and superdision costs. It is esimated that the increase in in6fratucture
would require the creation, at the end of the five-year program, of about 13,000 new posts in the four state, at
a cost of about US$29 million per annum, using an average of present salaries, in dollars, and representing
about nine percent of the SEP budget. Under the financial aangements of the project, such amount has to be
inscribed in the budget of SEP, that is still currently in charge of financing salaries of teachers.
4.            The institutional development program to be carried out in association with the environmental
component requires the expansion of the number of environmental specialists and associated staff that would
cost about US$1.2 million per annum at the end of the program.

Objectives &al Strategy
1.            The envirowne.w compent has the objectives of. (a) supporting dIe decentaition for tbe

significant remaining tmopical ran forest Biosphere Reserve. lTe component would support an evrnmena
program for all four states in support of the first two objectives, and, in the case of Chiapas an additional
Special Protection Program for the Selva Lacandona.
A. Envronmtal Prot        in the PNSP
2.    This subcomponent would support the implementation of the   vironmtal screening and assessment
measures, and contemplates the following activities:
- Considenng the specific PNS project related requiemtents and environental priorities in each

management of forest resources, protection of watersheds, etc.. To the greatest extent
possible, these studies should be cared out with due participation of the interested public,
universities and research institutions, environmental associations, etc.
Decentralization and Institutional Strengthenine
3.            The PNS program seeks to consolidate the process of decentralization, delegating to the States
and Municipalities the tasks of planing and of establishing their priorities for program  and projects in all
technical areas, including the envionmental and natural resource management one. To operaionalize the
environmental protection measures, the environment component would support a set of activities aimed at
gradual decentralization for the States of Hidalgo, Guerro, Oaxaca and Chiapas. Specifically,
(a)   The State Coordination mechanisms (COPLADES), State envirmental agencies and SEDUEs
in charge of implementing the PNS program, would receive the necesry technical support to
optimize the PNS contributions for the envionment sector, and to minimize any undesirable
impacts of the projects supported by PNS;
(b) the State agencies and SEDUEs would receive installations and equipment essential to the
monitorng of PNS supported lines of action and for related environmental proebtion;
(c)   strngthening of the system of proteted areas ia each State, through better protection of
existing areas and possibly also through desgnation of additional areas necesauy for the
conservation of ecosystems-with the objective of miniming negative impacts of econoic
development; and

- 1 08 -Pag 3 of 6
B.                    EMig   feciD th frs lacandonn
8.            The Selva Lacandona of astern Chiaps is tbe last significant tact of tropical forest in the
northern hemisphoe. In recent years, its contigowus core has been reduced at a very rapid pace to some
199,000 ha (out of a totl of some 580,000 ha) located in the Biospher of Montes Azules. The remaiing
sgnificavt st tacts which stl exist in the lger Lacandona region,are located mainly along the higher
dtitde. of mountain  ngws, and to the south in Marques de Comillas.
9.           ITe prest  size of the Landona core forest must be considered as the minimum size
necssy to prevent a prtid collaps of its highly endemic and exceptionally diverse eo-system. The concept
of Biosphero umdetlines the wodd wide importance of the Mont Azles reserve, and est h8es cerain
principles for its consevation; however, the Biosphere stus is not one of strict preservation.
10.           In all, estimates ae that the Lacandona forest may have been reduced to about 10 percent of
its origina size, as a result of large timber and rubber concessions sine the turn of the century until the 1920s
to more t   200 North American and British firms. Between 1930 and 1980 arge timber and rubber
concessimos were given to national firms (one of which had a conesion for 10 yeare to extract annually
90,775 m3 of timber per aum). While these concessions were grdually withdrawn over the last 20 years,
this did not result in any sigficant rduction of timber extraction and deforestation, as long as the two most
ntorious saw mills-th CompaNia Forestal de la Lacandona (COFOLASA) and the Compaflia TRIPLAY of
Palenque (TRIPSA) continued to operate a concession of 1,308,312 ha. Their operations were finally
discontinued in 1986.
11.           Whilo the last two decades saw a gradual decline of timber extraction, they also saw the
pallel iso of in the development of extensive livestock schemes, and of migration to the region for purposes
of a mdimentary slash and bun subsistence agriculture, and of Govemment settlement schemes, most
importantly in the frontier area of Marques de Comillas. According to data provided by the Chiapas
govemment, deforestation accelerated at an alarming rate: In 1875 tho region had 1,245,000 ha of pnmary
fores; in 1969 only 800,000. Between 1970 and 1975, 314,000 has were destroyed; between 1976 and 1980,
nother 128,000 ha; and between 1983 and 1988, another 143,000 ha-leaving a total of 580,000 ha of primary
forest cover in 1989. In summary, of the 665,000 has of foret cover destroyed over the last century, 585,000
were destoyed within the last 18 years.
12.           The Andona region has been a histoncally highly significant center of Mayan culture, and
comprises some 12 important archoological sites, all of them only partially excavated or not yet explored at all.
Today, the region is home to several Indian groups (some 400 Lacandones (who are believed to be a subgroup
of Mayans who migrated to the zone some 2 centuies ago), some 42,000 Tzeltales, 13,000 Tojolabales, some
8,000 Choles, and some 600 Tzotziles, and to Mostizuis/ migrant settlers from other parts of the country). In
1986 the total population of the Lacandona Region was estimated around 150,000. The Lacandona region at
present is relatively sparsely settled, with some 12 inhabitants per sq km, 35.4 percent of the area are under
ejidt land tenure, 47.2 percent are communal lands (of the Lacandona Indians), 9.2 percent are private preperty
and 8.2 pet are nationa lands. Successive legislation defined the Lcndona Indian, Forest and Biosphere
areas respectively: (a) a forest protection zone of 2,600,000 ha; (b) Forest Region for the Lacandona Indians of
1,300,000 ha; and (c) the Montes Azules Biosphere Reserve of 331,200 ha. In addition to agriculture-based
development, the Lacandona region has been in racent years characterized by exploratory activities for oil
drilling by PEMEX, by refugee problems and other national security concerns associated with its bordering on
Guatemala. Major hydro-power development on the Usumacinta and several other rivers has been proposed in
the region-but, because of its highly controversial nature, for the time being postponed.



-19-                 ~M.L.
Pagp 4 of 6
Recent Measure
13.           Because of the drastically accelerated deforetation rate and the growing threats of losses of
bio-diverity, the national and state govermets have take a series of measu  over the last two years to
break the pevailing trend, most importantly a presidential prohibition of futher timber extracfion and
agricultual land clearing. Increasing efforts have been made, among them:
- increased patrols and inspection to avoid illicit timber extraction;
- permits for the withdrawal of wood in 1989 were only given for timber felled prior to the 1988
prohibition, and need prior inspection by SEDUE, SARH and the State government;
- in 1990  l imber withdrawal has beea interdicted. Titmber companies have not been allowed
to tow already felled trees. While some contrand may still persist in the Guatinalan border
zone, this restriction has had a significant impact and saw mills in the region have been closed
down;
- promotion of protection against forest fires, and organization of farmnes for prevention
putposes. This has resulted in a virtual halt of forest fires in the Lacandona region and an 85
percent reduction state-wide;
- under the statewgovernment's coordination (acuerdo SARH/Gobierno Estatal), inter-institutional
integrafion of activities related to the forest sector. This state-level arrangement substitutes for
the previous federal Lacandona Forest Commission, given that the federal level has delegated
its responsibilities for the Lacandona Forest to the state;
- suspension of large scale catle schemes;
- a campaign of meetings and information to raise the level of consciousness regarding the
importance of preservation of the Iaandona Forest among local communities (the largest one
in August 1989 in Ocosingo);
- the cancellation of a variety of agricultural expansion authorization processes;
- a control of forest clearings (clearing of secondary growth (fallow))to the extent that several
ejidos have declared a disposition to relocate, provided they would be given altemative lands
elsewhere;
- workshops and meetings have been carried out in each community to establish farmers' needs
and joint programs of farmers and government authorities to attend to them;
- formulation of the medium term 'Programa de la Selva- (still being finished for the Cafadas
region);
creation of the Rural Development District of the Selva Lacandona, which began to function in
July 1990 as the single, collegiate entity for the planning, integration and evaluafion of
development activities;
initial implementation of programs and action plans for the development of the communities,
specifically, since 1989 programs in all sectors, with the objectivc  employment generation,
food production and income generation for the local populations without negatively affecting
the natural resource base. The projects initiated in 1989 include the construction of class
rooms, rehabilitation of rural roads, construction of the regional Hospital in Marques de
Comillas, electrification, construction of latrines, improvements in the supply of merchandise.
However, the most important changes have been introduced through the productive projects



Annex 9
- 110 -                                      Page 5 of 6
(rubber plntations, cocoa production improvemets, coffee replantings, a coffee proessing
(dying facility, installation of smal scale irigation equipment, insalation of Stoa
facilities, seasonal credit for annual crops (bes, com and chili), pig and poultfy units. animal
health/inoculation cmaigns, etc.). These projects all air at an intensification of land use in
already deforsd  eas, in order to elmin ate th need for new additional land clering;
- the first two watch posts have been constuct  (a total of 12 are planned) for the control of
timber logging and other floa, fauna and agnculture related items, at critical road
intersections;
- a natural esoue inventory has begun, but due to cloud cover conditions the aerial
photography could not yet be completed; a related semiar was held in San Cristobal de las
Cam in July 1990 and
- creation of a single state-level coordination Channel: the COPLADE subcommittee for the
Selva Lacandona.
14.            In 1990, new land clearings and burnings have been drastically decreased compared to the
levels of previous years. The 1990 investment plans call for an amplification of the types of programs initiated
in 1989, as well as the initiation of additional schemes (e.g., sheep units, cinnamon, cloves, pepper, plantains,
forest seedling production for afforestation purposes, bees, kitchen gardens), as well as stepped up patrol
programs in the Caiadas area.
The Proposed Special Proiect
15.            The Specid Project for the Lacandona Forest would support the ample program developed by
the Chiapas State Government for the protection of the Lacandona Forest. It would have the dual objective of
strict protection of the Montes Azules Biosphere and of promoting the conservation of remaining forest tracts in
the larger Lacandona Region through appropriate land use, social and economic development, protection,
research, communication, and inter-institutional coordination programs, and funding mechanismb. This program
would extend to the subareas of the Biosphere Reserve (331,200 ha134.6 percent), the Lacandona Community
(252,631 ha/26.4 percent), Marques de Comillas (203,999 haW21.3 percent), Zona Norte (87,867 hai9.2
percent) and Canadas (81,542 ha/8.5 percent). The project is smmarized in the following tables:

MEXICO
DECENTRALIZATION AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMEN1  JJECT
FOR THE DISADVANTAGED STATES
IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENTS. CAPACITY AND THE
INSTITUTIONAL DEVELOPMENT COMPONENT
A. Introduction
1.             The purpose of this annex is to provide an overview of the institutional arngements and reated
capacity issues and describe the objectives, approach and activities of the institutional development component.t
The analysis is first presented of four of the components of the project: rural roads; water supply; agriculture; and
education. The major issue in these is the capacity of the line agencies and local governments to implement the
planned works and to maintain them, especially given the context of increasing devolution of responsibiility to state
and municipal levels. In the areas of water supply, agriculture, education, and health, institutional development
activities will be conducted though the involvement of other Bank supported projects.2 In rural roads, capacity
issues are to be addressed through a program of technical assistance and training provided for within the project's
institutional developmtent component.
2.              It is important to note that in each of three of the sectors (roads, water supply and education);
project investments in infrastructure will primarily be contracted to the private sector. In these areas, concem for
institutional capacity rests with assessing the public sector agencies' ability to handle the more limited functions
related to planning, contracting and supervision of works.
3.             The institutional development component focuses on developing institutional capacity at the state,
municipal and community levels to plan, prepare, implement and operate and maintain development efforts. The
component will provide management and organizational development assistance to the inter-institutional planning
bodies at each level, the Sectetaria de Programacion y Presupuesto (SPP), and the municipalities and communities.
The assistance designated for the road sector will also address specific technical as well as organizational issues.
B. Proect Imglementation Arran_tmets and Capacity
Rual Roads Comoonent
4.             The program of rehabilitation, maintenance and construction of new rural and feeder roads in the
four states is the largest single component of the project.  In each of the four states, the Secretaria de
Comunicaciones y Transportes (SCT) centers,5 through the Comision Nacional de Carreteras Alimentadoras
(CONACAL), have primary responsibility for supervising the implementation of project activities. In each state,
however, there is one or two state agencies which, through an agreement with the SCT at the state level, will
execute portions of the work either directly or through contracts to the private sector. In addition to their role
during project implementation, the state agencies will be responsible for the ma;eance and continued construction
and rehabilitation of the rural road system following project completion. Annex 1 identifies the steps to be taken
in devolving responsibility for both construction and maintenance of rural roads from SCr to the state agencies and
to municipalities during the project.
1.         Specific targes for the institutional development component ae preented in Annex 1.
2.         In agrculbure the project is ongoing (PROCATI), in water supply and health the projects have been presented to the Board,
ad in education the project is under preparation.
3.          The SCT-Centers are directly responsible to the scr headquatters at the federal level.

7.             Significantamounts of new road constructionand rehabilitationofexisting roads will be contracted.
Initial plans for the states of Chiapas. Guero and Oaxaca, inckude the folowing: 68 perct of rural rd
construction and rehabilitation will he contracted, and 88 percent of feeder road construction and tehabilitation will
be contracted. Much of the maintenance activities will also be contracted. Forty-two percent of the rural road
maintenance will be contracted, as will 52 percent of the maintenance for feeder roads.
8.             According to the reports provided by each sktae, significant increases in state agency engineering
staff ..t planned. In Chiapas, the SDUC has increased its engineering staff from 7 to 40 from 1989 to 1990, an
inccease of 470 percent. In the next year it plans a further increase of 22, bringing the total to 62 (an aggregate
increase of 785 percent). Also in Chiapas, the CEC has increased from 15 to 19 engineers over the last year (an
increase of 27 percent). Over the next year it plans a funher increase of 15, bringing its total to 34 (au aggregate
increase of 127 percent).
9.            In Oaxaca, the CACU nas 32 engineers, copard with 24 in 1989. Over the next three yeas, it
will increase engineering staff by 42 (a total increase of 175 percent ftom 1989 to 1993), with 18 of those new
engineers being added in 1991. In Guerrero, MAQUINOP wiU increase its staff of 9 engineers by 32 in 1991 (an
increase of 355 percent), giving it a total of 41 engineers. The figures for curent and projected increase in
engineering staff for Hidalgo are not immediately available, but can be expected to also be significant.
10.           Institutional development activities will include tecbnical assistance and taining for each state
institution and for the municipalities to which some functions will be devolved. Over the four years of the project,
this will involve: (a) 50 persontmonths of TA per state, including two professional engineers and a training
specialist; (b) assistance for the preparation of updated SCT manuals and for the creation of a model for road use
cost/benefit analysis; and (c) 10 to 12 training activities per state. See Annex I for a list of targets for the
implementation of this institutional development program.

Water Supply Component
12.             The three year project plan calls for an investment of US $101.6 million in 1,893 drinking water
works and improvement projects in the four states over three years. Of this, Bank financing is for US $26.7 million
(on a 1:1 rmatching basis). These investments will serve localities with a population between 500 and 10,000
inhabitants.
13.            In Mexico, responsibility for the design, construction and operation of local water supply systems
has been devolved to state water agencies. In the four states participating in this project, these agencies are DAPA
(Chiapas), Comision de Agua Potable y Alcantarillado (CAPAEG-Guerrero), Cornision Estatal de Servicios
Publicos (CESP--Hidalgo), and the Comision Coordinadora de los Sistemas de Obras y Servicios de Agua Potable
y Alcantarillado del Estado (COCSOSA-PAEO--Oaxaca). Of those four agencies, thre (in Chiapas, Hidalgo and
Oaxaca) are line agencies of the state, and one (in Guerrero) is a state pstatal.
14.             These state agencies rarely do design or construction work themselves, depending upon private
sector contractors and the technical assistance of the federal Comisi6n Nacional del Agua (CNA). Thus CNA has
a significant technical and oversight role with regard to state water works.4 Tbese agenci  will receive technical
assistance and review from the CNA and will contract both design and construcion of works to the private sector.
Given this defined role of the public sector, the required capacity of the state agencies is Iimite to the following:

Agriculture Component
22.            The project budget for agriculture development activities in the four stdes over three years is
US$327 million. Of this investment, Bank fmancingwillbe US$41 milionforaricultualpromotionandextension
(including forestry and livestock), US$36 million for small scale irtigation, and US$11 million for agroindustry (on
a 1:1 matching basis).
23.            Govemnmental asistance to agriculture development is prinaily a fedea responsibility in Mexico.
The national Secretaria de Agricultura y Recursos Hidtaulicos (SARH) is the prinipal public sector institution
providing agricultural services to farmers in all states. SARH's operatios have been e5fectively deconcenate
to the state level, which operates the program through its offices at the district level (Distritos de Desarrollo Rural
Integrado-DDRI).s Within each district, there are three to five 'nural dvelopme support centers (CADERs).
The responsibilities of the DDRI, through the CADERs include: (a) technical assisnce in crop, animal and
foresty producton; (b) organization and training of farmers; (c) water resource management; (d) credit and crop
insurance; (e) artificial insemination and animal heath; (f) collection of agriculture statistcs; and (g) a range of
social progrms (e.g., nutntion, health, and family planning).
24.            The National Institute of Crop, Animal, and Forestry Research (INIFAP) is responsible for
agricultural research throughout Mexico. In each of the four states there is a state director of research, fumded
directly from the national INIFAP headquarters. Research is conducted from expeiment stations (e.g., Oaxaca has
three crops research stations, one forestry rescArch station and one livestock research station). The State Comnuittee
for Progammig and Evaluation of Research (COEPEI) provides overal guidance and review of the research
program. This comnmittee is made up of INIFAP and SARH representatives as well as representatives of the state
university and farmers organizatioDs.
25.           Irrigation development is handled by two federal agencies, the Comision Nacional de Agua (CNA)
and the Fidoicomiso de Riesgo Compartido (FIRCO). The CNA is responsible for public sector irrigation, and
focuses on the design, development and operation of medium and lage scle systems. Small scale irrigation
assistance to low income producers (i.e., mainly dug wells) is provided through FIRCO, a special fr's" providing
a mixture of grant, credit and technical asistance. Imgation assistance under the proect, as it is focused on small
community run systems, would be provided maiuly through FIRCO.
26.            As is the case with the other sectors, agriculture investments will be identified both by communities
and line agencies, and reviewed and approved by the COPLADEs. Once approved, work on the projects may be
contracted to the private sector directly by the communities or may be executed by the line agencies. Built into each
project budget is an additional five percent which is given to the executing agency (most likey SARH) to cover
_ohreimbursable costs.
27.            Ihere is evidence of a significant level of unused capacity in the SARH and INIFAP that can be
tapped by the project. In 1988 there were cuts in the investment budget for SAR! and INIFAP, without cuts in
personnel of a corrsponding number. For example, in Chiapas the authortzed SARH budget of US$27 million was
reduced to US$17 million. In the same year, budget cuts in DiNFAP/Chiapas' prognrm forced a reduction of
research activity by 50 percent.
