Institutional Devt.onment Component
28.           The purpose of the institutional development component of the project is to support the effective
planning and implementation of project investments and to contribute to the long term capacity of state and local
entities to continue directing their own development efforts.
29.           Overall responsibility for guiding project implementation is assigned to the federal Secreiriat of
Programming and Budgeting (SPP) and its four state offices. The project uses existing decentralized institutional
arrangements for the planning, budgeting and review of the investment program. This system has been established
under the guidance of the federal SPP, and involves the use of inter-agency and beneficiary planning commissions
at the state (COPLADE). regional (COPLADER) and local (COPLADEM) levels. Proposals that arise through this
participative planning procers are reviewed at the state level and then matching federal grant funds are provided to
the state through the Convenio Unico de Desarrollo (CUD).
30.           The SPP's state and regional offices provide direct support to this planning process by acting as
a secretariat to planning commissions at each level. In order to effectively implement project investments, Special
Coordinating Groups (SCGs) will be established by SPP at the federal, state and regional levels. Those Groups will
be responsible for providing administrative and technical support for project implementation. The federal SCG will
be staffed with eight professionals providing expertise in the areas of budgeting and progrmming, financia
administration, monitoring and evaluation, and technical support. Each state and regional SCG will also be staffed
in the same manner. Plans for the addition of other staff by federal and state institutions appear in Attachment 4.
31.           The state SCG staff will be dedicated exclusively to project activities, since the average workload
for each state for procurement, supervision, accounting and disbursement, would be about 300 projects per year,
with invoices for about US$21 million. In addition to providing technical assistance to the COPLADE in the review
and processing of project plans and disbursements, the state and regional SCGs will be responsible for providing
training and technical assistance to the 840 municipalities in the development of project proposals. Because of the
key role of the SCGs in project implementation, the creation and staffing of the SCGs is a conditidon of negotiation.
32.           The institutional development component will provide training and technical assistance in
management-related areas, primnanly focused on the planring commissions, the SCOs, and local govenmnts
(municipalities and localities). The component is budgeted for a total of US$11.35 million for technical assistance
and training, plus an additional US$3.2 million in assistance for the Indian Development Fund and US$4.2 for
studies (identified in pars. 34 below). Tle US$11.35 million will finance:
(a)    institutional development technical assistance (22 person months for each state and for the federal
SPP/SCG office, 50 r3rson months for each state road institution, the development of an updated
SCT manual for feeder roads, for a total of US$5.9 million), and
(b)    the design and conduct of on-site training courses addressing specific areas rehlted to project
implementation (e.g., project planning and appraisal, budgeting and expenditure control, and
project reporting and monitoring, engineering design and supervision, for a total of US$5.45
million).
33.           Central to the approach to institutional development will be the use of regular planning
workshops/seminars to provide an opportunity for operational level planang, the identification and resolution of
implementtation problems, and identification of specific training and technical assistance needs. In workshops held
twice a year, representatives of the four stats and the federat government (with Bank participation) will assess the
progress and current status of the project, prepare and re;we state investment programs, and cc -.re experience
among states. Similar workshops will also be held annually within each state for staff at state ana regional levels,
providing an opportunity for the SCG and the members of the COPLADEs and COPLADEMs to conduct similar
reviews.

34.           A major task of institutional development technical assitance (TA) will be to assist the SCOs in
the orgaization and conduct of these workshops, and the documentaon and follow-up of their results. Specific
institutional development and sector-reated tcchnical assistace and training will then be prog _tmmed to address
identified problem areas.
35.           Management-related taining TA will focus on the following areas: planning, finance, and
budgeting, project prepaation and appraisal, operation of management information systems for project monitoring,
and evaluation of project impact. Institutional strengtheni  activities for the water supply institutions will be
provided through the Bank supported Water Supply and Sanittion Sector Project (WSSP).
36.           Furthermore, in addition to the institutional development component, a provision has been made
for A series of studies, focused on: (a) compartive advantages and project identification, (b) rual employment and
specific needs for poor migrat households, (c) muketing of basic staples, and (d) socio-econic monitoring and
evaluation of the results of investments thugh the Indian Development Fund. The total amount budgetd for these
studies is US$4.2 million.

PROIECI INPUTS: INSTITJTIONAL DEVELOPMENT COMPONENT
1.            The project would help to bridge or mitigate institutional capacity gaps as identified against project
tasks during project pparation. For this purpose the project would finance a technical assistance and training
program of a combined budget of US$11.35 million. The creation of new administrative units has been limited as
much as possible to strengthen the capacity of existing organizational stuctues. The new units to be created (the
Federal and State Special Coordinating Groups) would be integrated in existing structures in such a way so that they
can become part of the permanent structures at the end nf project.
2.            Technical Assi    includes: (a) 22 staff months of organization and fimncial management
specialists for the central level and as many for each of the four States, for a total of 110 staff months; (b) 50 person
months for each state road institution for a total of 200 months; (c) assistance in the development of an updated SCT
manual for feeder roads. This assistance would help install modemn project management procedures; review norms,
inter-institutional relationships and internal distribution of functions so as to establish as early as possible, a smooth
flow of management functions within the Federal level, and between the latter and State and Municipal levels. The
specialists would work on necessary management tools (inter-institutional linkages) for making it explicit and
tansparent what project objectives (including evaluation indicators) resources and tasks (including monitoring and
organizing the reassessment of dsills especially at municipal level). Thereafter, they would help launch and deliver
training activities accordingly. The total cost of this sub-component amounts to US$5.9 million. These technical
assistance services would be acquired through a combination of ICB and LCB.
3.            Skill Ungradinu Activities include over 500 training activities, informative meetings, courses,
seminars, fellowships and possibly others to reach virtually all project tasks implementers at all administrative levels.
All trining activities have been identified against tasks. Skill gaps would be further identified and refined before
final training plans are designed. Most of the training activities are to be delivered by SPP and other Federal
Agencies' administrative and professional staff. Some specialized courses, especially to enhance municipal level
administrative skills, are to be delivered with the support of the Mexican Institute of Public Administration and
others with the assistance of technical assistance specialists. Training activities are to be evaluated through
interviews of trainees' supervisors to check on the bridging of their previously identified skill gaps. These training
activities would help bridge: (a) management skill gaps such as overall project management and coordination
functions; (b) professional skdll gaps such as planning, progrmming and budgeting, public finance control
(auditing), supervision activities; and (c) technical skill gaps such as systems operation, monitoring and evaluation,
budgeting, evaluating investment proposals sector-related skill gaps and others. Emnphasis would be on courses for
State and Municipal level staff and to a certain extent, for communities organizations' personnel and members.
Training activities at the municipal level should allow municipal capacity to move from the curret level to next
higher-capacity ranking by the end of project implementation. As this should be the States' responsibility, the
Federal level should ensure adequate transfer of technology at the State level before they can possibly strengthen
the municipal capacity. Total cost of this subcomponent has been estimated at US$5.45 million of which 30 percent
would be in foreign currencies.
4.            Eqgipment and Vehicles. The project would provide for the financing of office equipment,
furniture, technical equipment, the printing of information and diffusion material, computers and vehicles. This
would help equip State and Municipal offices up to their new responsibilities requirements. Detailed description
of this category of inputs is in the Component Workdng Papers. The cost of this sub-component would be included
in the state investment programs.

cawity:
o   Functional

(b)  determining M&E resource requirements: financial, technical and material (electronic, tanort,
etc.);
(c)  establishing standards and procedures for M&E operations: data collection, analysis, reportdig
and evaluation requirements and arrangements for the project. The project data ollection system
would integrate information from subproject selection through evaluation. Data gathering would
be coordinated with INEGI activities in Living Standards Measurements Survey (LSMS) and
organized to meet routine, semiroutine and special focus monitoring concerns for each of the
three project components;
(d) defining the analytic M&E agenda to determine subproject and component implementation
progress and performance (analysis of service delivery, feedback mechanisms, coordination of
inputs and policies, follow-up for corrective action);
(e) designing and managing the project M&E training program;
(f) managing overall project reporting requirements and preparing reports on progress, performance
and impact; coordinating these projects with the Financial Administration Section; and
(g)  designing in collaboration with the states, periodic evaluations and maging the quality standards
of these activities.
0    Techical1
(a) implementing data gathering in accordance with improved techniques (computerized database),
procedures, standards of periodicity and quality with a system for monitoring compliance;
(b)  using a variety of rapid, low-cost data collection techniques in preparation for analytic work;
(c)  applying electronic packages (including an expert system) for data processing, certain analysis
and linking with the project selection database; and
(d) providing techiical services to stutes and municipalities and arrangements for technical assistance.
6.           Technical SUonort Section. This Section would ensure adequate flow of specialist assistance to
the states and municipalittes for every sector considered in this Project, ensuring smooth and efficient
implementation through helping define modem management procedures and the delivery of management,
professional, technical and other trining activities as required in accordance with the skill gap analysis at all
levels of implementing staff and beneficiaries. Eight professionals would:
(a) teview and update of the institutional capacity analysis that was carried out during project
preparation;

7.            The State Level: Organizational Framework. At the state level, a special coordinating structure
similar to the federal one would be created in the office of the State Planning Secretariat, in addition to the
existing overall policy and fuiancial responsibilities of the state Coordinating Boards (COPLADEs) where the
Secretary of Planning is the chairman. The Project State Special Coordinating Group would be staffed with a
chief and four sections, each having two professionals, except for M&E. The state M&E Section would be
staffed by a minimum of three and preferably four professionals. The functions would encompass stte level
M&E: planning, data base development, monitoring according to the three levels of the system (see Annex 7),
arranging for, carrying out training, reporting and executing subproject evaluations carrying out evaluations.
The state M&E Section would develop with technical assistance, a single data base for subprojects and for
monitoring indicators of decentralization and institutional development activities. This section would also
benefit from the design and establishment of an expert system for project selection and monitoring. The state
technical support sections would draw upon the state M&E Sections to provide technical assistance and support
to the munic'palities, NGOs and beneficiary groups as and when needed. These sections would organize,
channel and even directly deliver assistance needed at the Municipal level for subproject generation, monitoring
and the assistance to beneficiaries' organizations functions. In cases where intensive assistance should be
delivered to groups of municipalities, state authorities will get organized in subregional assistance bases with
support from the federal authorities, to increase efficiency and follow-up activities. This may be the case in
Oaxaca where municipalities are very numerous. The state Special Coordinating Groups would be staffed as
much as possible with existing staff thereby increasing cost-effectiveness and sustainability of Project benefits as
it is implemented.

FLOW OF FUNDS
Participating Agencies
SPP/DGPPR                            Corresponding Bank           SPP/PNS Unit
SHCP/Treasury                                                     COPLADE
Banco Central                        NAFIN                        Executing Agencies
Provision of Funds
1.    The SPP General Directorate for Regional Planning and Budgeting (DGPPR) authorizes Executing
Agencies their Annual Workplans;
2.    SPP (DGPPR) requests Treasury to open a line of credit at corresponding Banks;
3.    Treasury through Central Bank (Banco de Mexico) opens line of credit at corresponding Bans and
replenish it in accordance with the Annual Budget;
4.    NAFIN with World Bank funds opens Special Account at Central Bank;
Executin2 Procedures
5.    Executing agencies prepare Cuentas por Lipuidar Certificado (CLC) and submit CLCs to COPLADE
for approval;
6.    COPLADE approves CLCs and submits to SPP Regional Delegate for registration and signature;
7.    After confirmation that works have been done COPLADE/PNS Unit authorizes corresponding Bank to
make payments;
8.    Corresponding Bank effects payments to suppliers;
Recovery of D-ocuments
9.    Executing Agencies prepare and maintain original invoices (Certificado de Gato [CGU);
10.    State PNS Unit prepares Summary Sheets of Expenditures (Relacion Certificada de Gastos [RCGI) on a
monthly basis;
11.    Monthly summary sheets of expenditures are signed by executing agencies and COPLADE are sent to
SPP in Mexico City;
Reimbursement
12.    SPP in Mexico ensures eligibility of RCGs and submits RCGs to NAFIN;
13.    NAFIN verifies and requests reimbursement from Special Account at Central Bank to the Treaury;
14.    Central Bank disburses from Special Account to Treary;
15.    NAFIN prepares Summary SOEs and submits to IBRD for reimbursement;
16.    IBRD through NAFIN replenishes the Special Account in the Central Bank in the amount of the SOEs
submitted.
N03h: Each CLC and each RCG needs three signatures: (a) executing agency; (b) COPLADE; and (c) SPP
Regional Delegate.

DECENTRALIZATION AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJEC

PROPOSED MONITORING AND EVALUATION SYSTEM
IntroductUon
1.          The first question a manager or decision-maker wants to know is: 'Was the project successful?
and if yes, 'What made it a success?" and if not, 'why?' A monitoring and evaluation system aims to address
these and siniilar kinds of questions.
2.          As stated in the objectives of the project, success would be the improved pubiic service delivery
and well-being of the target population in the four states. These improvements would be brought about
through the Policy Change, Institutional Strengthening and Investment Program components. Tb. project will
be judged a success or failure by the degree to which changes in these components are reflected in better public
service delivery and poverty alleviation. Improved public service delivery would be determined by increames in
coverage, access to and use of such services. Changes in well-being would be measured by improvements in
the poverty index, for which base line data exists. A basic proposition of the project is that great
decentralization, revenue sharing, and investment targeting would produce the conditions expected to exist at the
end of the project.
3.          Project success criteria for the three project components would be: (a)  _   measumnt
(indicators) related to increases in decentralized public expendituiw, block grants (revere sharing, and
investments to the four states; (b) institutional strengtening measurements related to increased capacity in
perfonming critical planning, management and monitoring and evaluation functions at the different levels
of public administration of the four states (see Annex 3); and (c) iovesttnmeasures related to greater equity
in financial distribution among the four states and programs and in accordance wi satisfactory executiog
agency performance.
4.          Determination of progress and performance according to the success criteria wil not be an easy
matter. Three different leveis of information gathering and analysis will be required. Level  would focus on
progress and performance in terms of basic monitoring requirements: financial and physical achievements as
compared to project plan. Disbursements viul be especially importnt to monitor given the annual disbursenme
conditionalities.
5.          This level of analysis would be similar to activities already carried out but it would be amplified
to incorporate variables related to sub-projects' objectives, completion performance, utilization of project outputs
and beneficiary participation (not only regarding inputs [labor, materials, funds] but especially for operations
and maintenance and cost recovery). At present, SPP/Mexico carries the dominant share of this wodrkoad. As
it is the most basic level of analysis and one which requires fumiliarity at closer rnge than is possible from the
federal level, major aspects of this function would be shifted to the states as part of the decetralization effort.
This will permit SPP/Mexico to devote more attention to tasks related to the second and third levls of analysis.
6.          Level 2 would determine the extent to which program/sector targets are being accomplishod.
Targets have been established to indicate thi extent to which the project expects to contribute to imprOVing
infrastructure (social and productive), social sorvice coverage and access, and the sustainability of productive
investments. Asssent of these contributions will require data collection and analysis somewhat beyond
presently performed activities. It would draw upon information souraes which are already in place but ae not
often integrated into normal project monitoring.
7.          Level 3 would examine project impact on service delivery and poverty alleviation. This wwld
involve sorting out what variables would be the most directly and indirectly related to project investment and



- 134 -                                   Pet 12.
Psge 2 of 8
implementation. It would require application of anaytic techniques, generlly associated with apphed research,
but tilring the to the present prject. It would also bring into consideration certain theoretical models of
poverty alleviation to help refine the analysis and guide its orietation. Among te questiona which would be
examined would be changes in agricultural production levels and adoption of new technologies; use of and
access to ad quality of water nd oducation services; operation and maintenae of itrigation systems; and
changes in agricultural commerialization. The proposed Monitoring and Evaluation System (M&E) defines
how information gathering and analysis for the thee levels would be caried out.
8.         TIis paper proposes a Monitorng and Evaluation System for the Regional Decentalization and
Development Project. It is based on a review of past experience with M&E under PIDERs 1, II, and mI and on
a cetain depth of understanding of the present SPP system. Furthermore, it builds on the project seection
system, proposed in n earlier paper. Three ar, weak in the past and pmeseat, would be improved: daft
collection on project execution beyond the financial and physical dimensions; analysis of project/progmm
progress, performance (especially in relation to investment outlays), and impact; and more rapid feedback for
planning and resource allocation. Given the policy changes expected in this project, M&E would also include
measures for monitoring and evaluating the effects such changes have on public service delivery and tesource
ail' ation as related to PNS fiaced projects.
9.          Specifically, the proposal defies: (a) the objective of the sysem; (b) information needs and
analytic activities at federal, state, and municipal/community level; and (c) opeational functions and procedures
of the system  It provides certain features to facilitate M&E execution. A M&E gstaty for data collection
and analytic work has been developed to permit a phased approach to extending the M&E coverage. All
proects would require monitoring accordig to Level 1 analysis described above; between 10-25 percent of the
projects would be monitored in relation to Level 2 analysis; and a smaller, but repreenative, proportion of
prects would be examined in terms of the Level 3 analysis. More details are provided on ndicatots according
to which progress, performance and the project's ultimate success (or failure) wwuld be determined Attamnt
1. A simple PrjA Monitog sheet would be introduced to ensute that data gateing includes information on
project objective, inputs (financial, matoeral and human resources), oututs (me4s), completion, utilization, and
beneficiary participation, i.e. the basic data reurements for Level 1 analysis. A       ecoim g
svstm is proposed whereby it would be possible to determine which kinds of ptojects are the most swcessful or
problematic.
10.        TMe proposal also envisions the comDuzeriaion of the system and its linking with the proiect
selection svstem. Subprojects could then be traced from initiation to completion over the entire project life,
including multi-year finncig.

o  Follow-up on results of annual review and planning workshop by monitoring implementation of planned
corrective actions.
o Finalize expert system installation for data processing and analytical work.
O Continue use of rapid appraisal techniques at state regional and state level in preparation for
analytic work.
o Gather data on project implementation according to improved standards of coverage, periodicity
and quality (e.g. percent of completed M&E forms with meaningfullusefuf information).
O Conduct an in field analysis of the extent and impact of the decentralization of services as per the project
targets.
O Prepare periodic (semi-annual) reports presenting analysis of: (a) project progress in relation to
planned financia outlays (Annual Operational Plans); (b) distribution of projects by region, state
and type of program; and coverage of poor population; (c) comparison of project perfonrance
with design; and (d) use of selection criteria by region, state, type of program, executing agency
and type of problems; and (e) impact of project, especially with egard to coverage and access by
the poor.
o Design, plan and conduct evaluation of third year project implementation for four states,
November-December 1993.

SUPERVISION PLAN
1.            In view of their innovative nature and complexity the supervision of the project requires a continuous
and high Bank staff input. Two workshops with SPPlBank paiticipation would take place. per year for a mnajor periodic
assessment of project implementation. Besides, it is planned that a staff position be created within the Bank office in
Mexico City to provide full time supervision to subproject execution in terms of civil engineering and economic
evaluation. The workshop that will take place in the first quarter of each year will provide an annual review of: (a)
investment programs; (b) technical assistance programs; (c) progress in policy measutes; and (d) review of recurrent cost
budgets comwected with the investment programs. In addition, we have agreed that a workshop will takc place in
September-October of each year to focus on specific points of policy implementation. Such workshops would also take
place in each of the states with the participation of all project states and federal agencies with responsibility for project
implementation. The workshops would have a duration of about three to five days. Bank missions would carry out field
supervision which would be defined by each sector specialist. It would include on-site visits to a set of projects,
interchange with executing agencies, review of disbursement, appraisal, procurement and auditing tules on a sample of
projects.

DECENTRALIZATION AND REGIONAL DEVEIOPMENT PROJECT
FOR THE DISADVANTAGED STATES
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR STUDIES TO BE UNDERTAKEN UNER THE PROJlCI
Com2arative Advantages and Project Identification
Qjective: The study will provide information: (a) to improve the fonmulation of a strategy of development; (b)
to clearly bottlenecks to productive investments and policies to improve the environment of business activity;
and (c) to identify packages of investment projects in productive sectors that show a promising rate of retun.
The study will concentrate on the project states of Chiapas, Guerrero, Hidalgo and Oaxaca.
Problem: Despite the efforts done in the projr^t preparation and in the formulation of curent development
plans, there is an urgent need to identify types of productive activities at the farm and non-farm level that could
ehnce productivity for the populations of those poor states. These studies will help to identify the
comparative advantages of these regions and translate them into concrete project ideas. Furthermore, while
productive activities are the realm of private sector initiative there is an urgent need to build a bank of
productive projects that could be used in enterprise development by the different banking and entbepren'
associations.
Pbe: The study will comprise three phases: (1) a preliminary phase that will define a common methodology
(comprising standardized questionnaires) to survey local businesses and identify suces stories and bottlenecks;
(2) field work will comprise a set of case studies that would study some successful enterprises and subsectors
with the view of identifying the crucial human and location factors that explain such success; (3) a final report
would integrate the above substudies and indicate avenues for policy action in order to create a climate inductive
to enterprise development.
imwmgm tation: The implementation of the above study will require for phase (I) a business conslting firm or
a team of highly qualified and experienced specialists (perhaps a business school) that would study each of the
sates. Phases (2) and (3) could be implemented by groups of university sudents closely monitored by buainess
administration professors in the state universities with some guidance from one or tWo business experts. The
last phase could be implemented also either by a consulting firm or a team of experts. Thesa  busines
associations should be invited to participate as sounding board and possible users of the pret packages.
Cost Phase I - US$400,000; Phase 2 - $500,000; Phase 3 - US$400,000. Total - USS1.3 million.
Rural Enplovnent and Specific Needs of Poor Miarant Households
Objvey~: To provide information on: (a) socioewnomic groups that due to lack of assets and difficulties in
findig gainfil opporunities are etemely vulnerable;  ) problems of seasonality, droughts or other natural
calamities that can result in extemwe human sufferance; and (c) the design of public employment programs tht
wouldd help to absorb temposrar unemployment or raise incomes for populatons under permanent
underemployment. In addition, the study will provide information within originating areas on: (a) the patterns
of belivior (specially migration) of the different socio-economic gtoups and identify their sources of income;
(b) the potential impact on intrahousehold welfare (wife and children) and on the conducting of productive
activities; and (c) progams to improve the welfare of poor households in those gwps and ways to provide
them with basic social services.

Problem  In quite a number of studies in different coutnes one of the major cause of extreme povety is the
lack of ginful occupations between crpping seasons. SeverlW countries have implmented program  of pubic
employment specially for maintenance of mral infrastuctu  with some succes. Given the widesprad lsck of
mainteance in Mexico in that infraructure desigaing some public works progran  could help solve thes
problems. Furthermore, several studies have indicated that a lare part of the popuations (with a large
participation of indigenous populations) in the four poor states have sevl members that migrate for part of the
year either to the north of Mexico or the US/Caiada duing the cropping SeSoD. Sometims the whole
household moves temporarily, other times the absoece of the men originates that the women have to take over
the farming or other eonomic activities. The needs of those populatios are quite different and very few
welfare programs targeted to them exist.
Pha:  The study wiU comprise four phases: (1) review of studies on the Mexican rura labor mdets and
social progmms that have been itmplmted in Mexico and design of a household suvrey (perha  based on the
LSMS) to characterize its functionig in some regions of Mexico; (2) implm on of the surver, (3) final
report comprising analysis of part (1), intepretation of results of survey and rs   for programs to
help these populations increase their income and provide them with minmum soci services.
QW: Phase I - US$150,000; Phase 2 - US$1 million; Phse 3 - US$250,"00. Total - US$1.4 million.
Markeftg of Basic StaVles
Q fbikti: To provide information on: (a) the fuctioning of regional markets of maize and beas, price
fluctuations and market stucture; (b) the role of parastatals in regulating the markets and the emergence of
farmes' associations; and (c) the existence of market-power and market imperfections.
Ploblem: Maize and beans continue to be a major staple for Mexican poor households living in mral areas.
The impact of the recent adjustment measures mainly in prices (and exchange rsAes) and wages on the income
and welfare of the poor runi households have not boen sfficiently studied. Finally, a major resistance
cinues to exist for deregulating the makets for naize and beans and there is not enough knowledge on the
role of some makdet intermediaries.
Pbae: the study will comprise four phases: (1) review of the literate; (2) study of the impact of the
adjustment umsu   on such fanmers using a simple simulation technique; (3) study of the reponal madkets of
maize and beans in four slected regions and simulation of the mpat  of regulatory meaures in prices and
farmers' income; and (4) repot mmarizing the main findings of the above and policy r ons.
Qg; Phase 1 - US$150,000; Phase 2 - US$150,000; Phase 3 - US$400,000; Phase 4 - USS200,000. Total
US$0.9 million.
lIndian Dmaeome9n=  Funds:
62onemig Monitoi n d Eauto
Qhjbie e: The study will provide a systemic evaluation of. (a) te peformace of the lndian Development
Funds (IDFs) n incraing giculturl nd atisa production and allevating poverty unog the indigenous
population in the four project states; (b) the managea  and finncial peforma    of the IDFs in different
regions and among different othnotinguiatic groups; (c) the relations of the IDFs to other souces of credit for
indigenous prodwers (e.g., moneylenders, state and commrcial banks, etc.); (d) the effects of the IDFs on
household income, technology innovation, divson of labor, migrtion paerss, com_mty oganization, etc.,
among fund participants and comm_ities; (e) the outreach of the funds in terms of status of beneficiaries witlin
the indigenous comunties; and (f) ways of impvg thdo manial pformne, soci outreach nd socio-
cmomic impact of th IDPs.

Problem: Access to credit, the design of cultlly-aptopriate technica assistsnee and trining, and excessive
paternalism have be  pesistent problems in the delvey of sevices to Mexico's relatively large and poor
indigenous population. To remody this situation, the National Indian Institute (INIM, with tihds from the
PRONASOL program, has initiated an Indian Development Fund program. 1he program has established a
number of Indian Development Funds (IDFs) in each of the regions where there ae large concentats of
ladian population. Each fund is an autonomous entity; comprised of indigeous communities, ejidos,
cooperatives, and artisan groups; administered by a democrally-elected Board of Dirctors; and provided
with technical assistance and training by special technical support teams.
As a new institution, there is still relatively little known about the functioning of the IDFs and their capacity to
increase the organizational capacity and productive potenti of the indigenous population. For this reason, a
systematic monitorig and evaluatiou study wil be designed by researchers contcted by INI. Four
independent research teams will be organied for eah of the pm*ct states Te research teams wil use a
common anthropologica and paticipatory methodology for mtonioring, evluating and improving the
performance and outreach of the IDF program.
EPae  The study will be caried out in three phases over a three year period: (1) design of the study,
conttacting of the four research teams, and testing of the research methodology (six months); (2) conducting of
fieldwork, household and survey research, preliminary data analysis and writing of interim project report (24
months); and (3) writing of final project report (6 months).
Cod: Approximately US$600.000.
